@AndStrats @usairforce @DeptofDefense @Andrews_JBA The photo is from the service yesterday, Feb. 7, and shows the U.S. Air Force Honor Guard who helped conduct military funeral honors for Maj. Gen. Harris.
Harris was laid to rest alongside her husband, Lt. Col. Maurice Harris, who was buried at #ArlingtonNationalCemetery in Jan. 16, 1996. Their daughter, Tenecia, received the U.S. flag from Harris' service.		#HonorThem
Commissioned in 1965, Harris was also the first African American woman to attain the rank of Maj. Gen. in the U.S. military. She served for 32 years before retiring in 1997.

This deciduous holly is planted for its long lasting reddish berries and is a valuable emergency winter food for many birds.  Song birds rely on it when all other tastier food has been devoured or covered with snow.
Arlington National Cemetery is an accredited Level III Arboretum with more than 600 varieties of trees and shrubs.  One, the Ilex verticillata, or Winterberry, is pictured here in the winter sun. https://t.co/v49DfJbKAV

@dodpaa personnel began exhuming the remains from the Punchbowl for analysis in 2015. Using mitochondrial DNA analysis, anthropological &amp; dental analysis, along with circumstantial evidence, Bruesewitz' remains were finally identified March 19, 2018. #HonorThem
The remains of the deceased crew were recovered and interred in the Halawa and Nu-uanu Cemeteries. After an attempt at identifying the remains in September 1947, they were buried at the National Memorial Cemetery of the Pacific (NMCP), known as the Punchbowl, in Honolulu.
On Dec. 7, 1941, Bruesewitz was assigned to the battleship USS Oklahoma which was moored at Pearl Harbor when the ship was attacked by Japanese aircraft. After sustaining multiple torpedo hits, the USS Oklahoma quickly capsized and resulted in the deaths of 429 crewmen.
Earlier today, on the 77th anniversary of his death during the attack on Pearl Harbor, @USNavy Seaman 1st Class William Bruesewitz was laid to rest in Section 60 of Arlington National Cemetery. https://t.co/r6smhVmVDE

Today We Remember‚Ä¶.		U.S. Army Maj. Walter Reed, died on this day in 1902. Best known as a pioneer bacteriologist, he led experiments establishing mosquito transmission of yellow fever. Reed's breakthrough in yellow fever research is widely considered a milestone in biomedicine. https://t.co/U96aT2ZRSZ

Rear Adm. Herbert E. Schonland eventually retired from active service in 1947, and was promoted to the rank of rear admiral in recognition of his distinguished combat record.
Recognizing that Lt. Cmdr. Bruce McCandless was skillfully conning the cruiser, Rear Adm. Herbert E. Schonland remained at his post below, where his efforts were critical to saving the ship. For his extreme heroism and courage on this occasion, he was awarded the #MedalOfHonor.
Rear Adm. Daniel J. Callaghan and the ship's captain, Cassin Young, were among those killed, leaving then Cmdr. Herbert E. Schonland as senior surviving officer. Though command thereby devolved on him, he was already engaged in vital damage control efforts.
In the bloody Nov. 13, 1942, night action in Iron Bottom Sound, the San Francisco was the flagship of Rear Adm. Daniel J. Callaghan's task force. 		While battling a greatly superior Japanese force the heavy cruiser San Francisco was badly damaged by enemy gunfire.
Rear Adm. Herbert E. Schonland graduated from the @NavalAcademy in June 1925. During #WorldWarII, Schonland's ship, the San Francisco, played an important role during the first three months of the long and difficult fight for Guadalcanal.

The memorial is located in Section 1 next to the @NatlParkService Ranger Station.
A cairn, the traditional Scottish monument honoring the dead, can be an informal heap of stones or may take a more orderly construction. In this instance, the 270 stones fit together to form a circular tower eight feet wide at the base and tapering to a height of 11 feet.
Eleven of the 270 dead were on the ground. The passengers and crew included people from 22 countries. Among them were 189 Americans, including 15 active duty military and 10 veterans.
The ill-fated flight was en route from Frankfurt, Germany, to New York via London's @HeathrowAirport. At 7:02 p.m., 27 minutes after leaving London, the plane exploded, raining fragments on the city of Lockerbie, including an entire wing and engines.
The #LockerbieCairn is a gift of the people of Scotland to the people of the United States, financed entirely through private donations.
The #LockerbieCairn, through its 270 blocks of red Scottish sandstone, memorializes the 270 lives lost in the terrorist attack on the United States when Pan Am Flight 103 was bombed Dec. 21, 1988, over Lockerbie, Scotland.

Gen. Maxwell Taylor is laid to rest with his wife, Lydia Happer Taylor, in Section 7A, Grave 20.		#HonorThem
Information for this thread courtesy of https://t.co/yZemgFnZfy.
Gen. Maxwell Taylor then pursued a civilian career, first as chairman of the board of the Mexican Light and Power Company and later as president of the @LincolnCenter for the Performing Arts in New York.
After retiring from active duty in July 1959, Gen. Maxwell Taylor criticized U.S. strategic planning and joint organization in The Uncertain Trumpet, which was published in 1960. This book influenced Kennedy's decision to adopt the strategy of flexible response.
Gen. Maxwell Taylor was a diplomat of the mid-20th century, who served as the fifth Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff after having been appointed by the President John F. Kennedy.
In honor of #NationalAuthorsDay, today we're highlighting Gen. Maxwell Davenport Taylor, former Ambassador to Vietnam and Joints Chief of Staff who wrote several books including The Uncertain Trumpet.		(Portrait courtesy of @DeptofDefense) https://t.co/zvRFB2uJtl
Cmdr. Arthur M. Preston is laid to rest with his wife Elizabeth McBride Preston in Section 3, Grave 1847-A-1.	   	#HonorThem
Cmdr. Arthur M. Preston's exceptional daring and great personal valor enhance the finest traditions of the U.S. Naval Service.
Under continuous fire for two and a half hours, Cmdr. Arthur M. Preston successfully achieved a mission considered suicidal in its tremendous hazards, and brought his boats through without personnel casualties and with but superficial damage from shrapnel.
Under continuous fire for two and a half hours, Cmdr. Arthur M. Preston successfully achieved a mission considered suicidal in its tremendous hazards, and brought his boats through without personnel casualties and with but superficial damage from shrapnel.
Increasingly vulnerable when covering aircraft were forced to leave because of insufficient fuel, Cmdr. Arthur M. Preston raced PT boats 489 and 363 at high speed for 20 minutes through shell-splashed water and across minefields to safety.
Aided by an aircraft smokescreen, Cmdr. Arthur M. Preston finally succeeded in reaching his objective and, under vicious fire delivered at 150-yard range, took the pilot aboard and cleared the area, sinking a small hostile cargo vessel with 40-mm. fire during retirement.
Twice turned back while running the gauntlet of fire from powerful coastal defense guns guarding the 11-mile strait at the entrance to the bay, Cmdr. Arthur M. Preston was again turned back by furious fire in the immediate area of the downed airman.
Volunteering for a perilous mission unsuccessfully attempted by the pilot's squadron mates and a PBY plane, Cmdr. Arthur M. Preston led PT-489 and PT-363 through 60 miles of restricted, heavily mined waters.
Cmdr. Arthur M. Preston's citation read: For conspicuous gallantry and intrepidity at risk of his life above and beyond the call of duty, while effecting the rescue of a pilot shot down less than 200 yards from a strongly defended Japanese dock and supply area, on Sept. 16, 1944.
Cmdr. Arthur M. Preston was awarded the #MedalOfHonor for a heroic rescue off Halmahera Island while in command of Motor Torpedo Boat Squadron 33 during the Morotai operation, Sept. 16, 1944.

Fleet Adm. William F. Halsey is laid to rest with his wife Frances Grandy Halsey in Section 2, Grave 1184. Halsey is laid to rest next to his father, @USNavy Capt. William Frederick Halsey and mother, Anne Masters Brewster Halsey.	 	#HonorThem
Want to read more about Fleet Adm. William F. Halsey? There is an extended biography available at https://t.co/dZQFOl3A1m.
Information for this thread courtesy of the @USNatArchivesat https://t.co/OWjBadyvEO.
On Sept. 2, 1945, the Japanese signed the surrender document on the deck of Fleet Adm. William F. Halsey's flagship, the #USSMissouri. He became an Admiral of the Fleet (five stars, comparable to General of the Army) in late 1945.
In the Pacific during #WorldWarII, Fleet Adm. William F. Halsey was given increasing responsibility for actions that would push the Japanese back to their homeland.
In 1934, Fleet Adm. William F. Halsey was offered command of the Saratoga on the condition that he go to flight school and be rated as an observer. He agreed, but insisted on training as a pilot even though he was twice as old as the pilots then in training.
Fleet Adm. William F. Halsey's first assignment after graduating from the @NavalAcademy in 1904 was aboard the #USSKansas as part of the Great White Fleet. During #WorldWarI, he was in the Atlantic, commanding torpedo boats and destroyers before getting command of the #USSShaw.

Maj. Gen. George B. McClellan is buried in Riverside Cemetery in Trenton, New Jersey.		#HonorThem
Information for this thread of the @natlparkservice at https://t.co/94Daj7emnw.
#McClellanGate was constructed in the 1870s as a tribute to Maj. Gen. George B. McClellan, whose wartime headquarters were located in @ArlingtonNPS.
Following the establishment of Arlington National Cemetery in 1864, the grounds were enclosed. 		Several gates provided pedestrian and vehicular access. The main entrance to the cemetery was located where #McClellanGate towers 30 feet above the roadway.
In 1864, Maj. Gen. George B. McClellan was nominated for President by the Democratic Party but lost the election. He did serve as governor of New Jersey from 1878-1881. 		On October 29, 1885, McClellan died in Orange, New Jersey.
After the Battle of Antietam, Maj. Gen. George B. McClellan was ordered to turn over his command to his good friend Ambrose E. Burnside and to go home to New Jersey to await further orders. 		They never came.
Maj. Gen. George B. McClellan was outranked only by General-in-Chief Winfield Scott. 		He reorganized a disjointed and poorly disciplined Army, pushing it into the field in response to Gen. Robert E. Lee's invasion.
Maj. Gen. George B. McClellan had proven himself to be an efficient organizer with strong personal magnetism. For this reason, and some successes in West Virginia, President Lincoln approved him major general in the regular Army.
When the #CivilWar began, Maj. Gen. George B. McClellan was living in Ohio, where he served as president of the Ohio and Mississippi Railroad.
In 1857, Maj. Gen. George B. McClellan resigned his commission of captain in the 1st Cavalry to become chief engineer of the Illinois Central Railroad, where he occasionally worked with a lawyer named Abraham Lincoln.
His time observing the #CrimeanWar resulted in the development of the "McClellan saddle," which was standard equipment in the @USArmy until mechanization eliminated horses in 1942.
Maj. Gen. George B. McClellan's other accomplishments include surveyor of possible transcontinental railroad routes. 		As a member of a board of officers, he was sent abroad to study the armies of Europe and observe the #CrimeanWar.
Maj. Gen. George B. McClellan was also an instructor at @WestPoint_USMAfor three years.
Upon graduation, Maj. Gen. George B. McClellan was appointed second lieutenant in the @USACEHQ. 		In the #MexicanWar, he won brevets of first lieutenant and captain for his zeal, gallantry and ability in constructing roads and bridges over routes for the marching army.
#DidYouKnow @WestPoint_USMA's class of 1846 contributed 20 generals to the #CivilWar's Union and Confederate Armies?
In 1846, Maj. Gen. George B. McClellan earned the distinction of graduating second in his class of 59 students. 		(He was outranked in his class only by Charles S. Stewart, who later would serve under him as a captain of engineers.)
Maj. Gen. George B. McClellan entered school at the age of five. He attended private schools and a prep school before entering @WestPoint_USMA in 1842. At the age of 15, he was the youngest of the #WestPoint arrivals that year to seek a place as fourth classman.
Maj. Gen. George B. McClellan was born in Philadelphia, Pennsylvania, Dec. 3, 1826. He was the third of five children born to Dr. George and Elizabeth (Brinton) McClellan. His family moved within the upper ranks of Philadelphia society.
#TodayIRemember @USArmy Maj. Gen. George B. McClellan who died #OnThisDay in 1885. 		Who are you remembering today?		#HonorThem		(McClellan Gate photo by Elizabeth Fraser. Authority was granted Nov. 20, 1970, to place a 12-inch-by-18-inch bronze plaque over the carved inscription and reads:		This Monument Was Erected in 1938	and Rededicated in 1971	To Commemorate Devoted Service	To Country and Humanity By	Army, Navy, and Air Force Nurses
On July 13, 1970, #USNavy Capt. Delores Cornelius, deputy director of the Navy Nurse Corps, requested authority to install a bronze plaque over the existing inscription on the #NursesMonument. The inscription at that time simply read 'Army and Navy Nurses.'
The granite statue of a nurse in uniform, sculptured by Frances Rich, honors the nurses who served in the U.S. armed forces in #WorldWarI, many of whom rest among the hundreds of nurses buried in Section 21 -- also known as the 'Nurses Section.'
Against a background of evergreens, a heroic size white marble figure looks out upon the @USArmy, @USNavy and @USAirForce nurses who so valiantly earned their right to lie at Arlington.

Pvt. John B. Hutson is laid to rest with his wife Isabel Crawford in Section 15A, Grave 33-A-LH.		#HonorThem
Biographical information courtesy of @KentuckyAlumni at https://t.co/aJFx2tJwCz
The @USDA sent Pvt. John B. Hutson to Berlin, Germany, as a tobacco specialist in the Foreign Agriculture Service with his primary duties to be to survey European tobacco production.
During #WorldWarII, Pvt. John B. Hutson served as President of the Commodity Credit Corporation and was then Assistant Secretary General in charge of administration of the United Nations during its organization period, 1946-47.
After earning a Bachelor of Science degree in Agriculture from the @universityofky in 1917, Pvt. John B. Hutson was awarded the Master of Science degree from the @UWSystem in 1925 and the Doctor of Philosophy degree from @Columbia University in 1930.
Pvt. John B. Hutson was born in Murray, Kentucky, Sept. 7, 1890.
The @UnitedNations is an international organization founded in 1945. It is currently made up of 193 member states.

Maj. Gen. Adolphus Greely is laid to rest in Section 1, Grave 129.		#HonorTHem
From 1887-1906, Maj. Gen. Adolphus Greely was the @USArmy's Chief Signal Officer and brought the #SignalCorps into the age of modern technology. During his tenure he introduced the radio, automobile, and the airplane into military use.
Maj. Gen. Adolphus Greely was an explorer who survived two years of arctic winters without being resupplied in 1881. He also founded the @NatGeo Society (@InsideNatGeo), and was awarded the #MedalOfHonor when he was 91 years old.

Pharris is laid to rest with his wife Elizabeth Lee in Section 13, Grave 16281.	   	#HonorThem
Biographical information for this thread courtesy of the @USNavy at https://t.co/OFqz8vt30n.
Initially Lt. Cmdr. Jackson C. Pharris was awarded the #NavyCross, but was later upgraded to the #MedalOfHonor. During his #WorldWarII service, he also earned the #PurpleHeart and #GoldStar.
By Lt. Cmdr. Jackson C. Pharris' inspiring leadership, his valiant efforts and his extreme loyalty to his ship and her crew, he saved many of his shipmates from death and was largely responsible for keeping the California in action during the attack.
Rendered unconscious 2x by nauseous fumes + handicapped by painful injuries, Lt. Cmdr. Jackson C. Pharris persisted in his efforts to speed up the supply of ammo and repeatedly risked his life to enter flooding compartments and drag shipmates who were submerged in oil to safety.
With water and oil rushing in where the port bulkhead had been torn up, and many crewmembers overcome by oil fumes, the ship without power and listing heavily to port as a result of a second torpedo hit, Lt. Cmdr. Jackson C. Pharris ordered the ship fitters to counter flood.
Quickly recovering, Lt. Cmdr. Jackson C. Pharris acted on his own  initiative to set up a hand-supply ammunition train for the antiaircraft guns.
In charge of the ordnance repair party on the third deck when the first Japanese torpedo struck almost directly under his station, Lt. Cmdr. Jackson C. Pharris was stunned and severely injured by the concussion that hurled him to the overhead and back to the deck.
During the Japanese attack on Pearl Harbor, Dec. 7, 1941, Lt. Cmdr. Jackson C. Pharris was onboard the #USSCalifornia.
#TodayIRemeber @USNavy Lt. Cmdr. Jackson C. Pharris who died #OnThisDay in 1966.		Who are you remembering today?		#HonorThem		(Headstone photo courtesy of Arlington National Cemetery. Portrait courtesy of @USNHistory.) https://t.co/6mSPjToZGM

Col. John W.  McGuinness is laid to rest in Section 34, Grave 138.		#HonorThem
The Bronze Star Medal with valor is awarded for acts of heroism involving conflict with an armed enemy and the Air Medal is awarded for meritorious achievement while participating in an aerial flight. It can be awarded to a member of any of the branches of the armed forces.
Col. John W.  McGuinness served during the #VietnamWar and #PersianGulf War. During his service he earned the #BronzeStar Medal with valor and the #AirMedal with three oak leaf clusters.

Rear Adm. Samuel G. Fuqua is laid to rest with his wife Edna Agnes in Section 59, Grave 485.	   	#HonorThem
Information for this thread courtesy of @USNHistory at https://t.co/fG9rJN1iqL.
After serving as chief of staff of the Eighth Naval District, Rear Adm. Samuel G. Fuqua retired from active duty in July 1953, receiving at that time the rank of rear admiral on the basis of his combat awards. He died in Decatur, Georgia, Jan. 27 1987.
Following the war, Rear Adm. Samuel G. Fuqua served in other staff positions, and from 1949-1950 commanded the destroyer tender Dixie.
From 1943-1944, Rear Adm. Samuel G. Fuqua was assigned to #GuantanamoBay, Cuba, and  attended the @NavalWarCollege. 		Fuqua was operations officer for Commander Seventh Fleet in 1945, helping to plan and execute several amphibious operations in the Philippines and Borneo area.
During most of 1942, Rear Adm. Samuel G. Fuqua was an officer of the cruiser Tuscaloosa.
For Rear Adm. Samuel G. Fuqua's distinguished conduct and heroism at that time, he was awarded the #MedalOfHonor.
After the ship's forward magazines exploded, Rear Adm. Samuel G. Fuqua was the USS Arizona's senior surviving officer and was responsible for saving her remaining crewmen.
Though knocked unconscious by a bomb that hit the ship's stern early in the attack, Rear Adm. Samuel G. Fuqua subsequently directed firefighting and rescue efforts.
After service at the Naval Training Station, Great Lakes, Illinois, from 1939-1941, Rear Adm. Samuel G. Fuqua returned to #USSArizona as the ship's damage control officer and first lieutenant, and was on board during Japan's Dec. 7, 1941, raid on Pearl Harbor.
Rear Adm. Samuel G. Fuqua served in other ships and shore stations during the mid-1930s, and was commanding officer of the minesweeper Bittern in the Asiatic Fleet from 1937-1939.
Following graduation and commissioning in June 1923, Rear Adm. Samuel G. Fuqua served in the battleship Arizona, destroyer McDonough and battleship Mississippi before receiving shore duty at San Francisco, California, from 1930-1932.
After a year at the @Mizzou and #WorldWarI service in the @USArmy, Rear Adm. Samuel G. Fuqua entered the @NavalAcademy in July 1919.
#TodayIRemember @USNavy Rear Adm. Samuel Glenn Fuqua who was born in Laddonia, Missouri, #OnThisDay in 1899.		Who are you remembering today?		#HonorThem		(Headstone photo courtesy of Arlington National Cemetery. Portrait courtesy of @USNHistory.) https://t.co/JsyEWSoVX7

Cmdr. Asaph Hall is laid to rest with his wife Mary Estella and daughter Katharine Cockrell Hall in Section 7, Grave 8067.		#HonorThem
Biographical information for this thread courtesy of the @UMich at https://t.co/rQty1tvM8U.
Astronomy ran in the family - Cmdr. Asaph Hall's father and namesake discovered the two moons of Mars in 1877. His mother Chloe Angeline Stickney Hall was a mathematics professor who helped her husband with much of his research.
From 1889-1892 Cmdr. Asaph Hall was Assistant Astronomer at the Naval Observatory. From 1892-1905 he was Professor of Astronomy and Director of the Observatory at @UMich. In 1905, he returned to the Naval Observatory. He retired in 1929, fell ill and died months later in 1930.
From 1882-1885, Cmdr. Asaph Hall was assistant at the U.S. Naval Observatory in @WashingtonDC. From 1885-1889 he taught at @Yale Observatory, at the same time doing advanced work leading to the degree of Doctor of Philosophy, which he received from Yale in 1889.
Cmdr. Asaph Hall was born in Cambridge, Massachusetts, Oct. 6, 1859. He initially attended college at Columbian University at George Washington University in 1876 before transferring to @Harvard University, where he graduated from in 1882.
In honor of #AstronomyDay, today we are highlighting @USNavy Cmdr. Asaph Hall Jr., who was a professor of mathematics and an astronomer. 		#HonorRememberExplore		(Photo courtesy of the @Michiganensian, @UMich's yearbook) https://t.co/wmaKqbnleJ

Maj. Gen. Leonard Wood is laid to rest with his wife Louise Adriana and his son, Leonard Jr. in Section 21, Grave 10.		#HonorThem
Information for this thread courtesy of https://t.co/OmvpCV9Y0v.
#DidYouKnow there is a @USArmy installation in Missouri, @FortLeonardWood, named for Maj. Gen. Leonard Wood?
Maj. Gen. Leonard Wood served as the Army's Chief of Staff from 1910-1914. His last position of service was a governor general of the Philippine Islands, which Spain had ceded to the U.S. after the Spanish-American War. He held this position until his death in 1927.
At the outbreak of the #SpanishAmericanWar, Maj. Gen. Leonard Wood commanded the 1st U.S. Volunteer Cavalry, better known as the #RoughRiders. His second in command, Theodore Roosevelt, took over the regiment when Wood was promoted.
A warrior and a surgeon, Maj. Gen. Leonard Wood  graduated from @Harvard University and began his military service as a contract surgeon during the Apache Indian Wars in the 1880s, earning the #MedalOfHonor for valor.
Maj. Gen. Leonard Wood was a distinguished Soldier whose service to his country spanned 40 years.
#TodayIRemember @USArmy Maj. Gen. Leonard Wood who was born #OnThisDay in 1860.		Who are you remembering today?		#HonorThem		(Headstone photo Rachel Larue, Arlington National Cemetery. Portrait courtesy of @NPG) https://t.co/MKyEefJCwo

There are 20 plaques adorning the bottom of the sculpture, listing what many historians consider to the 20 most decisive battles during #WorldWarII.
The five men are in uniform and represent the armed forces during #WorldWarII. One of the women represents the nurse corps during #WWII, and the other woman represents #RosieTheRiveter.
The 12-foot high, 1,800 pound bronze sculpture includes a guardian angel holding a dying unknown Soldier, a globe, and seven bronze figures at the top including five men and two women.

Dr. Anita Newcomb McGee is laid to rest in Section 1, Grave 526-B.	   	#HonorThem
Information for this thread courtesy of @NLM_news at https://t.co/W5XfuT4zsB.
For her services, the Japanese government honored Dr. Anita Newcomb McGee with the Imperial Order of the Sacred Crown. 		Later she lectured on hygiene at the @UCBerkeley, and for the rest of her life, divided her time between several homes and overseeing her son's education.
In 1904 during the #RussoJapaneseWar, Dr. Anita Newcomb McGee offered the Society's services to the Japanese government. Spending six months in Japan working beside nurses in that country, McGee was designated "superior of nurses" with the rank of an army officer.
Dr. Anita Newcomb McGee also helped found the Society of Spanish-American War Nurses in 1900 to look after the interests of the @ArmyNurseCorps, and served as the organization's president for the next six years.
Dr. Anita Newcomb McGee helped the @USNavy establish a nursing corps two years later, and in 1899 wrote a manual on nursing for the military.
At the end of the #SpanishAmericanWar, Dr. Anita Newcomb McGee drafted the legislation that established the U.S. @ArmyNurseCorps.
After assembling approximately 1,600 highly qualified nurses, Dr. Anita Newcomb McGee was appointed acting assistant surgeon general of the @USArmy, becoming the only woman authorized to wear an officer's uniform.
Dr. Anita Newcomb McGee created a special committee of the @todaysdar to screen nurses and then offered their services to Army Surgeon General George M. Sternberg.
Dr. Anita Newcomb McGee petitioned Army Surgeon General George M. Sternberg to permit only fully qualified nurses to serve.
In 1898 with the outbreak of the #SpanishAmericanWar, Dr. Anita Newcomb McGee discovered through friends that Army Surgeon General George M. Sternberg intended to use nurses at base hospitals for the first time since the #CivilWar.
For the next few years, Dr. Anita Newcomb McGee operated a private practice before withdrawing from clinical work around 1895 to pursue other interests.
After earning her doctor of medicine degree from Columbian University (later @GWtweets) in 1892, and gaining experience in political organization, she completed an internship at the Women's Clinic in Washington, D.C., and studied gynecology at Johns @HopkinsMedicine.
In 1888, Dr. Anita Newcomb McGee married William John McGee, who supported her decision to attend medical school soon after they were married.
Educated at home and in elite private schools in @WashingtonDC, Dr. Anita Newcomb McGee was later able to travel to Cambridge, England, and @unige_en, Switzerland. She pursued interests in history and genealogy, even writing and delivering lectures on these topics.
Dr. Anita Newcomb McGee's parents were both respected intellectuals and academics and her  mother, in particular, encouraged her daughter to pursue diverse  academic subjects.
Dr. Anita Newcomb McGee's childhood and her family's social status afforded her a multitude of educational and professional opportunities.
Dr. Anita Newcomb McGee was instrumental in organizing the 1,600 nurses who served during the conflict and wrote the Army Reorganization Act of 1901, which established the @ArmyNurseCorps as a permanent unit.
In 1898, during the Spanish-American War, Dr. Anita Newcomb McGee was named acting assistant surgeon general of the @USArmy, making her the only woman permitted to wear an officer's uniform.

Tech. Sgt. Dave Richardson is laid to rest with his wife Ruth in Columbarium Court 2, Section K, Column 8, Niche 4.		#HonorThem
Information for this thread courtesy of the @LibraryCongress at https://t.co/WNHdyTn9Bg.
Tech. Sgt. Dave Richardson retired in 1982 to take up volunteer work in Washington D.C. 		He passed away Jan. 25, 2005.
Tech. Sgt. Dave Richardson worked for @Time and Life Magazine in India, Germany, Britain, the  Middle East, and Mexico, and @USNews and World Report as chief correspondent for South America and chief correspondent for Europe.
Following #WorldWarII, Tech. Sgt. Dave Richardson stayed abroad as a civilian foreign correspondent covering some of the century's major news stories.
Tech. Sgt. Dave Richardson's decorations include the @USArmy's #LegionOfMerit, #BronzeStar, #CombatInfantryBadge, plus the Valor Medal of the National Headliners Club.
Tech. Sgt. Dave Richardson's most notable assignment was as the only reporter to walk with the legendary ranger force Merrill's Marauders through their entire 600-mile march behind Japanese lines from India to China.
Tech. Sgt. Dave Richardson took part in 10 battles on land, sea, and in the air in two theaters of war, Southwest Pacific and China, Burma, India.
Tech. Sgt. Dave Richardson became a familiar name to members of the armed forces during #WorldWarII as one of the principal combat correspondents for the global GI-run magazine Yank, The Army Weekly.
The volunteer force first known as Galahad and later dubbed Merrill‚Äôs  Marauders skillfully adapted to fighting in the jungles of Burma. 		Their guerrilla tactics were brilliantly executed to defeat an enemy force with superior numbers and firepower.
Infantry Soldiers in China-Burma-India served with scant resources and a sense of physical isolation.
#TodayIRemember @USArmy Tech. Sgt. Dave Richardson, who as a combat correspondent covered Merrill's Marauders, the famed #WorldWarII infantry unit that was founded #OnThisDay in 1943. 		Who are you remembering today?		#HonorThem		(Courtesy photos) https://t.co/btaueEVPmj
Sgt. Thomas J. Kelly is laid to rest with his wife Wilma in Section 7A, Grave 125.	   	#HonorThem
Information for this thread courtesy of @AMEDDMuseum at https://t.co/ruNhLUuR7Q.
Read firsthand of Sgt. Thomas J. Kelly's experience at https://t.co/6o5Am1uMHo.
Sgt. Thomas J. Kelly refused to leave his platoon until the attack had been resumed and the objective taken. His gallantry and intrepidity in the face of seemingly certain death saved the lives of many of his fellow soldiers and was an example of bravery under fire.
In all, Sgt. Thomas J. Kelly made 10 separate trips through the brutal fire, each time bringing out a man from the death trap. He guided and encouraged seven other casualties who were able to crawl by themselves to escape the hail of fire.
Sgt. Thomas J. Kelly was forced to crawl, dragging the injured behind him for most of the 300 yards separating the exposed area from a place of comparative safety.
Ordered to withdraw, then Cpl. Thomas J. Kelly reached safety with uninjured remnants of the unit, but, on realizing the extent of casualties suffered by the platoon, voluntarily retraced his steps and began evacuating his comrades under direct machine-gun fire.
Sgt. Thomas J. Kelly's platoon, committed in a flanking maneuver, had advanced down a small, open valley overlooked by wooded slopes hiding enemy machine guns and tanks, when the attack was stopped by murderous fire that inflicted heavy casualties in the U.S. ranks.
Sgt. Thomas J. Kelly was a medic during #WorldWarII and was awarded the #MedalOfHonor for actions performed April 5, 1945, during an attack on the town of Alemert, Germany.

Proud families displayed flags in homes, businesses, schools and churches bearing a blue star for each family member serving in the military. Families stitched a gold star over the blue star to honor those members who died during military service.
The term "Gold Star" originated during #WorldWarI.
Gold Star Mother's and Family's Day is observed in the United States on the last Sunday of September. The U.S. Congress recognizes this as the day when the U.S. honors surviving parents and families of fallen service members -- specifically those who died while on active-duty.

Harry Blackmun is buried in Section 5, Lot 40-4.		#HonorThem
Harry Blackmun suffered a fall at age 90 and underwent hip-replacement surgery. He died March 4, 1999, from complications arising from the surgery.
In 1997, Harry Blackmun appeared in the 1997 #StevenSpeilberg movie Amistad as a Supreme Court justice who delivered the decision that African captives who had commandeered the schooner Amistad were born and remained free men.
Harry Blackmun resigned from the court Aug. 3, 1994, and was succeeded by Justice Stephen G. Breyer.
Harry Blackmun announced his opposition to capital punishment in all  circumstances shortly before his resignation after 24 years on the  Supreme Court.
Harry Blackmun's role on an increasingly conservative court often left him in the minority on opinions later in his career. He was the sole dissenting justice from a ruling that Haitian refugees could be intercepted in their boats and forcibly returned to Haiti without a hearing.
Although joined in by six other members of the court, the abortion rights decision brought Blackmun criticism and threats, none of which he ducked. Rather he pressed on his fellow jurists to understand the consequences of their rulings and deal with their real-world effects.
Harry Blackmun is best known as the author of the 1973 decision in Roe vs. Wade that extended the right of privacy in reproductive matters to the decision of women whether to carry a pregnancy to term.
'What does equal protection mean? What is cruel and unusual punishment? I would hope that  I have grown in that respect over the years. But as far as shifting  from conservatism to liberalism, I don't believe I have done that.'
In grappling with the issues that came before the court, Harry Blackmun evolved an understanding of 'the great phrases' of the U.S. Constitution that drew him a long way from his beginnings.
Prior to that Harry Blackmun was resident counsel for the @MayoClinic and Mayo Association for nine years.
Harry Blackmun was recommended to the president by Chief Justice Warren Burger, a childhood friend, and was heralded by Nixon as a 'strict constructionist' when plucked from the Eighth Circuit Court of Appeals where he had served for 11 years.
Harry Blackmun went on earn a Bachelor of Laws from @Harvard_Law School in 1932.		Nominated to the #SupremeCourt after President Nixon's first two choices were rejected by the Senate (Clement Haynesworth and Harold Carswell), he reputedly said being picked third kept him humble.

Gen. @GeorgeCMarshall is laid to rest in Section 7, Grave 8198.		#HonorThem
For nearly 20 years Gen. @GeorgeCMarshall was a major U.S. leader, militarily, politically and morally, and he is still widely admired today.
In 1953 Gen. @GeorgeCMarshall was awarded the @NobelPrize for Peace for his role in proposing, encouraging legislative action, and supporting the European Recovery Program (known as the #MarshallPlan).
After the war, Gen. @GeorgeCMarshall was named special ambassador to China (1945‚Äì1947), Secretary of State (1947‚Äì1949), President of the American @RedCross (1949‚Äì1950), and Secretary of Defense (1950‚Äì1951).
During #WorldWarII, Gen. @GeorgeCMarshall as @USArmy Chief of Staff (1939‚Äì1945) was the most important military figure in the U.S. military establishment and of great significance in maintaining the Anglo-American coalition.
Gen. @GeorgeCMarshall‚Äôs career touched on many of the key events of the 20th century, and he is the only person to have served as the @ArmyChiefStaff during #WorldWarII, as Secretary of State following #WWII, and as the Secretary of Defense during the #KoreanWar.

Warren Earl Burger is laid to rest with his wife Elvera Stromberg Burger in Section 5, Grave 7015-2.	   	#HonorThem
Warren Earl Burger is perhaps best known for authoring the unanimous opinion that required Nixon to surrender @WhiteHouse tape recordings and papers that had been subpoenaed for use in Watergate cover-up trials.
Warren Earl Burger sought to improve the administration of justice in the U.S. as much through reform of administrative procedures and court management and efficiency as through the more than 250 decisions he authored and the votes he cast + cases he had brought before the court.
Warren Earl Burger came to @WashingtonDC to serve as Assistant Attorney General, Civil Division, of the Justice Department and was named by President Dwight Eisenhower to the U.S. Court of Appeals for the District of Columbia in 1955.
Warren Earl Burger earned his degrees at night, working days to support himself. In private practice in the Twin Cities, he taught law and became involved in electoral politics floor-managing the 1948 +1952 presidential bids of Harold Stassen at the Republican Nat'l Conventions.
Warren Earl Burger, the 15th Chief Justice of the United States, served 17 years in office before resigning to accept appointment as the unpaid chairman of the Commission on the Bicentennial of the U.S. Constitution.

.@AmericanLegion posts are organized into 55 departments: one each for the 50 states, along with the District of Columbia, Puerto Rico, France, Mexico and the Philippines.
Membership swiftly grew to over 1 million, and local posts sprang up across the country. Today, membership stands at over 2 million in more than 13,000 posts worldwide.
The @AmericanLegion was chartered as a patriotic veterans organization. Focusing on service to veterans, service members and communities, the Legion evolved from a group of war-weary veterans of #WorldWarI into one of the most influential nonprofit groups in the United States.

Maj. Walter Reed is laid to rest in Section 3, Grave 1864.		#HonorThem
Information for this thread courtesy of https://t.co/erkyEIKhNJ.
Although Maj. Walter Reed died more than a century ago, his name and legacy persist. The @WRBethesda in Bethesda, Maryland, is named for him.
Just a year later, Maj. Walter Reed described how #YellowFever, one of the most dreaded tropical diseases, is transmitted.
In 1898, Maj. Walter Reed investigated the typhoid fever epidemic, expanding our understanding of how this disease spreads.
In 1893, Maj. Walter Reed became curator of the Army Medical Museum (now the @MedicalMuseum) and professor of clinical microscopy at the Army Medical School (now the @WRAIROfficial).
Maj. Walter Reed was a pioneering bacteriologist and led experiments establishing mosquito transmission of #YellowFever. His research into the causes of typhoid and yellow fever has saved countless human lives.
#TodayIRemember @USArmy Maj. Walter Reed, who was born #OnThisDay in 1851.		Who are you remembering today?		#HonorThem		(Portrait courtesy of the #USArmy. Headstone photo by Elizabeth Fraser, Arlington National Cemetery) https://t.co/aDZsZuHtcl

Rear Adm. David Griggs is laid to rest in Section 7A, Grave 81.		#HonorThem
At the time of his death, Rear Adm. David Griggs was in flight crew training as pilot for STS-33, a dedicated @ DeptOfDefense mission, scheduled for launch in August 1989. He died June 17, 1989, near Earle, Arkansas, in the crash of a vintage #WorldWarII airplane.
During the flight, Rear Adm. David Griggs conducted the first unscheduled extravehicular activity (spacewalk) of the space program. The spacewalk lasted for over three hours during which preparations for a satellite rescue attempt were completed.
In September 1983 Rear Adm. David Griggs began crew training as a mission specialist for flight STS 51-D, which flew April 12-19, 1985.
In July 1970, Rear Adm. David Griggs began working at the @NASA_Johnson Space Center. Starting out as a research pilot, he was eventually selected as an astronaut candidate in 1978.
Rear Adm. David Griggs graduated from the @NavalAcademy in 1962 and went on to become a decorated combat aviator during the #VietnamWar.

William Rehnquist is laid to rest with his wife Natalie Cornell in Section 5, Grave 7049-LH.		#HonorThem
On Sept. 3, 2005, after a long battle with thyroid cancer during which William Rehnquist declined to step down from the bench but was forced to be absent from many oral arguments, he passed away in his suburban Virginia home.
After his wife passed away in 1991, Rehnquist spent the last 14 years of his life as a widower, passing his time with quiet hobbies.
As chief justice, William Rehnquist won the respect of his colleagues through his efficient management of court affairs. Rehnquist also revealed a moderation in his views by voting with liberals to protect gay rights and free speech.
A Democratic Senate overwhelmingly confirmed his nomination. In 1986, President Ronald Reagan nominated William Rehnquist as chief justice.
Richard Kleindienst sought William Rehnquist for the position of deputy attorney general in the Justice Department's Office of Legal Counsel. When Justice John Marshall Harlan retired in 1971, the Nixon administration chose Rehnquist as Harlan's replacement.
During that time, William Rehnquist befriended Richard Kleindienst, another attorney from Phoenix. When Richard Nixon rose to the presidency a few years later, he appointed Kleindienst deputy attorney general of his administration.
Eventually William Rehnquist began participating in politics and becoming a Republican party official. Rehnquist campaigned for Republican presidential candidate Barry Goldwater during the 1964 elections.
After completing a clerkship under Supreme Court Justice Robert Jackson, William Rehnquist moved to Phoenix and began working for a law firm.
#DidYouKnow Sandra Day O'Connor, who would eventually serve with William Rehnquist on the #SupremeCourt, graduated third from that same class?
A distinguished student, Rehnquist was elected to @PhiBetaKappa in 1948. He continued his education at @Harvard where he received earned a master of arts degree -- 2 years later. William Rehnquist returned to @Stanford Law School in 1950; he graduated at the top of his class.
Like many U.S. citizens in his generation, William Rehnquist attended college after #WorldWarII with the support of scholarship money from the #GIBill. At @Stanford, he earned both a bachelor and a master of arts degree in political science.
#WorldWarII erupted before William Rehnquist had a chance to complete his education and the future chief justice enlisted in the #AirForce branch of the @USArmy as a weather observer. He served in North Africa.
William Rehnquist was born in Milwaukee, Wisconsin, Oct. 1, 1924. The son of a paper salesman, Rehnquist grew up in a heavily conservative household in which popular Republican leaders such as Alf Landon, Wendell Wilkie, and Herbert Hoover were idolized.
#TodayIRemember Chief Justice of the Supreme Court William Hubbs Rehnquist, who died #OnThisDay in 2005.		Who are you remembering today?		#HonorThem		(Headstone photo by Rachel Larue, Arlington National Cemetery. Portrait courtesy of the U.S. Senate.) https://t.co/0oAGdsBOaY
#DidYouKnow there are casualties of and veterans of the Revolutionary War laid to rest at Arlington National Cemetery?		For a listing, visit https://t.co/elA8Yc0n9L.
This turning point in the American Revolution prevented the British from dividing New England from the rest of the colonies, and it was the deciding factor in bringing active French support to the U.S. cause.
British Gen. John Burgoyne's surrender followed battles with U.S. Gen. Horatio Gates near Saratoga in September and October 1777. With the British losing men and defenses during both engagements, Burgoyne retreated with a weakened army to Saratoga, where he surrendered to Gates.
#OnThisDay in 1783, the Treaty of Paris officially ended the #RevolutionaryWar.		Pictured is the surrender of British Gen. John Burgoyne at Saratoga, New York, Oct. 17, 1777. 		#HonorRememberExplore		(Painting by John Trumbull, courtesy of the @USCapitol) https://t.co/f8fyQsnrd2

Maj. Gen. Philip Kearny is laid to rest in Section 2, Grave S-8.		#HonorThem
#DidYouKnow there are two equestrian statues in the cemetery? 		One for Maj. Gen. Philip Kearny and one for @BritishArmy Field Marshal Sir John Dill.
Maj. Gen. Philip Kearny was killed in action in the 1862 #BattleOfChantilly. Kearny was buried at Trinity Churchyard in New York. In 1912, his remains were exhumed and re-interred at Arlington National Cemetery under the equestrian statue in his honor.
When the #CivilWar broke out in 1861, Maj. Gen. Philip Kearny returned to the Army and was appointed a brigadier general. The Army had been reluctant to restore his commission due to his disability, but realized the need for experienced combat officers like Kearny.
After the war, Maj. Gen. Philip Kearny served in Army recruiting in New York City. While there, he was promoted to major but later resigned his commission.
During a battle in the Mexican-America War, Maj. Gen. Philip Kearny led a daring cavalry charge and suffered a wound to his left arm which was later amputated.
Maj. Gen. Philip Kearny was known for his fearless character in battle earning him the nickname "Kearny the Magnificent." He is also known for his leadership in the #MexicanAmericanWar and #CivilWar.
#TodayIRemember @USArmy Maj. Gen. Philip Kearny Jr. who died #OnThisDay in 1862. 		Who are you remembering today?		#HonorThem		(Grave photo by Rachel Larue, Arlington National Cemetery. Portrait courtesy of @NatlParkService) https://t.co/f2BlTJGZdc

The discovery of oil on Trinidad in 1910 added another important export. Independence was attained in 1962. The country is one of the most prosperous in the Caribbean thanks largely to petroleum and natural gas production and processing.
Manpower was replaced with the importation of contract laborers from India between 1845 and 1917, which boosted sugar production as well as the cocoa industry.
According to the @CIA's World Fact Book, First colonized by the Spanish, the islands came under British control in the early 19th century. The islands' sugar industry was hurt by the emancipation of the slaves in 1834.

Dr. Albert Bruce Sabin is laid to rest in Section 3, Grave 1885-RH. 		#HonorThem
Dr. Albert Bruce Sabin also received the Presidential Medal of Freedom from Ronald Reagan in 1986, which is highest award that an American civilian can receive.
Dr. Albert Bruce Sabin received the National Medal of Science in 1970 for his work up to and including the development of the oral polio vaccine.
Dr. Albert Bruce Sabin began testing a new polio vaccine on humans, starting with his own family.
During the same time period, Dr. Albert Bruce Sabin discovered that the polio virus attacked and multiplied in the intestines, as opposed to the respiratory tract as was previously believed.
The first inactive polio vaccine, which required an injection administered by a doctor, was created in 1954 by Dr. Jonas Salk.
In the 1930s, the outbreak of polio, which led to paralysis or death, had reached epidemic proportions both in the United States and around the globe.
#DidYouKnow Dr. Albert Bruce Sabin invented the oral polio vaccine?

James Parks is the only person buried in Arlington National Cemetery who was born on the property. 		He is buried in Section 15E, Grave 2.		#HonorThem
James Parks worked for the @USArmy from 1861 to 1929 by working as a grave digger and maintenance man for the cemetery. When he died on Aug. 21, 1929, he was granted special permission to be buried at Arlington National Cemetery.
James Parks still lived on Arlington Estate when Secretary of War Edwin M. Stanton signed the orders designating Arlington as a military burial ground.
The first graves in #ArlingtonNationalCemetery were dug by Parks, a former Arlington Estate slave. Parks was freed in 1862 under the terms of the will of his former owner, George Washington Parke Custis.
#TodayIRemember James Parks, who died #OnThisDay in 1929.		Who are you remembering today?		#HonorThem		(Headstone photo courtesy of Arlington National Cemetery. Portrait and other content courtesy of @NatlParkService) https://t.co/SLYNMYOh0W

Col. Stuart A. Roosa is laid to rest with his wife Joan Barett Roosa in Section 7-A, Grave 73.		#HonorThem
Information for this thread courtesy of https://t.co/UOW06jqPOv and https://t.co/pLLsKUunC8.
Col. Stuart A. Roosa served as backup command pilot for the Apollo 16 and 17 missions, and was assigned to the space shuttle program until his retirement in 1976, where he went on to work in the private industry. He died Dec. 12, 1994, due to complications of pancreatitis.
In April 1966, Col. Stuart A. Roosa was one of 19 astronauts selected by @NASA. He was a member of the astronaut support crew for the #Apollo9 flight, and completed his first space flight as a command module pilot with #Apollo14.
Following graduation from the Aerospace Research Pilots School, Col. Stuart A. Roosa was an experimental test pilot at @EdwardsAFB, California, from September 1965-May 1966.
Col. Stuart A. Roosa was a maintenance flight test pilot at Olmstead Air Force Base, Pennsylvania, from July 1962-August 1964, flying F-101 aircraft.
Col. Stuart A. Roosa was assigned as a fighter pilot at Langley Air Force Base (now @JBLEnews), Virginia, where he flew the F-84F and F-100 aircraft. He served as Chief of Service Engineering at Tachikawa Air Base, Japan.
Col. Stuart A. Roosa joined the #AirForce in 1953 and is a graduate of the Aviation Cadet Program at Williams Air Force Base, Arizona, where he received his flight training commission.
Col. Stuart A. Roosa spent several summers in the early 1950s as a smoke jumper with the @ForestService parachuting in to fight forest fires.
Col. Stuart A. Roosa graduated with honors and a Bachelor of Science degree in Aeronautical Engineering from the @CUSystem.
.@USAirForce Col. Stuart A. Roosa was born #OnThisDay in 1933 in Durango, Colorado. 		#HonorThem		(Photo courtesy of @NASA) https://t.co/DNrGoTmSL6
#HonorThem https://t.co/XAsbidLQci

Learn more about #VJDay at https://t.co/Lq8UxBMvs9.
The #WorldWarII memorial was dedicated in tribute to the legacy of the "Greatest Generation" May 29, 2004.
The #WorldWarII Memorial honors the service of 16 million members of the U.S. armed forces, the support of countless millions on the home front, and the ultimate sacrifice of 405,399 U.S. citizens.
Not far from the cemetery is the #WorldWarII Memorial. Pictured is the memorial's 24th panel, which depicts the emotions of V-J Day. It is one of 24 bronze bas-relief panels that flank the memorial's entrance and tell the story of the United States' experience in the war.
#VEDay, or Victory in Europe, occurred May 8, 1945, when Germany surrendered, thereby allowing the U.S. to concentrate on defeating Japan in the Pacific theater. 		The formal treaty of surrender between Japan and the Allies was signed Sept. 2, 1945 aboard the #USSMissouri.
More people were killed and more cities all around the globe were destroyed during #WorldWarII than in any other war. 		Due to the time difference, the United States typically commemorates V-J Day Aug. 14.
Known as #VJDay, the Allies‚Äô Victory in Japan is commemorated #OnThisDay marking the end of #WorldWarII, the most catastrophic war in history. 	   	#HonorRememberExplore	   	(#WWII Memorial photo courtesy of @NatlParkService) https://t.co/hW4gKcpVSY

Some code talkers translated messages into their native languages and relayed them to another tribal member. 		Others developed a special code within their languages that they used in combat to send important messages.
American Indian code talkers were communications specialists. 		Their job was to send coded messages about troop movements, enemy positions, and other critical information on the battlefield.
‚ÄúCode talkers,‚Äù as they came to be known after #WorldWarII, are twentieth-century American Indian warriors and heroes who significantly aided the victories of the United States and its allies.
The United States military asked them to develop secret battle communications based on their languages‚Äîand the country's enemies never deciphered the coded messages they sent.
More than 12,000 American Indians served in #WorldWarI‚Äîabout 25 percent of the male American Indian population at that time. 		During #WorldWarII, when the total American Indian population was less than 350,000, an estimated 44,000 Indian men and women served.
In honor of #NavajoCodeTalkersDay, today we're highlighting the American Indians who used their languages as weapons during #WorldWarI and #WorldWarII.		Pictured are members of the Navajo Code Talkers Association at the #MemorialAmphitheater July 6, 1983. 		(@USArmy photo) https://t.co/31UFsNadpF

Earlier today, @WIMSAtweets opened their newest exhibit, "Women Marines - Proudly Serving 1918-2018." 		Next time you're at the cemetery, stop by the #WIMSA Memorial and help honor 100 years of women #Marines.		#HonorRememberExplore
Information for this thread courtesy of https://t.co/xSyUvTtI1z.
And like the other 304 women who were enlisted into the Marine Corps during #WWI, Opha May Johnson never left her clerical duties.
By the time of her exit from active service in 1919, Opha May Johnson had risen to the rank of sergeant and was working for the quartermaster still. 		She was the highest ranking woman in the @USMC during the war.
Now as Pvt. Opha May Johnson, she would continue to serve the @USMC in Washington, D.C., in the office of the Quartermaster General of the Marine Corps.
At the time of her enlistment, Opha May Johnson had been working as a civil service employee at Headquarters Marine Corps in Washington D.C.
And so, by being the first in line on Aug. 13, 1918, at a @WashingtonDC recruiting station, Opha May Johnson, originally from Kokomo, Indiana, became the first woman enlisted in the U.S. Marine Corps.
Secretary Josephus Daniels approved Maj. Gen. George Barnett's request six days later on Aug. 8. Word was spread quickly via word of mouth and the media channels of the era, and five days later enlistments of women began.
The key reasoning being there were any number of clerical and administrative duties that women could perform as well as men, and that by doing so, those men could be freed to perform field service instead.
On Aug. 2, 1918, Commandant of the Marine Corps Maj. Gen. George Barnett sent a request to Secretary of the @USNavy Josephus Daniels for women to be enrolled in the Marine Corps to replace men in clerical duties.
The solution came in the form of the creation of the Marine Corps' Women's Reserve.
With as many men enlistees as possible needing to be trained for combat  on Europe‚Äôs western front, the #MarineCorps realized that it still needed able bodies capable of performing the administrative tasks men had performed up until then.
When Opha May Johnson enlisted in the @USMC, the United States was in the middle of #WorldWarI. 		#WWI100
Good timing, luck, and a paradigm shift in history are often the graces which allow someone to be commemorated for important accomplishments. 		In the case of Opha May Johnson, the three graces allowed her to be the first woman to join the U.S. #MarineCorps.	   	(@USMC photo) https://t.co/u2Nr28N1uE

Charles Blair died in an aircraft crash Sept. 2, 1978. He is laid to rest in  Section 2, Grave 4966, with his wife Maureen Fitzsimmons Blair, more commonly known as Maureen O'Hara. Blair also served in the @USNavy.	 	 #HonorThem
Information for this thread courtesy of https://t.co/KG4KoNUDUw .
While Charles Blair‚Äôs solo flight over the pole in itself was exciting, his time in the Air Force developing advanced celestial navigation systems is a legacy he should be remembered for.
Charles Blair‚Äôs navigational systems made it possible to compute a fix to within  100-200 feet. 		In the pre-GPS era, this kind of technology was  revolutionary.
Charles Blair's dual-navigation systems were never actually used with jet fighters in the Soviet Union, but they were put into place during the Vietnam War in the F-105 Thunderchief.
The other system he worked with was  pre-computed navigation astrotrackers. Naval officer and navigation  expert P.V.H. Weems worked to create the pre-comp calculations for  Charles Blair.
Charles Blair used two different navigation systems to address this problem. 		The first was an inertial navigation system, which helped navigate by using a computer, motion sensor, and  rotation sensor to calculate the direction and speed of the plane.
Charles Blair became an active duty colonel  in the Air Force and was assigned to the task of taking a jet fighter  plane and turning it into a long-distance nuclear delivery system that  the U.S. could use against the Soviet Union.
Charles Blair's stunt was a major success,  and signified to the U.S. military that there were some big changes to  be made to current navigation systems.
On May 29, 1951, Blair set out in his  single-seat P-51 (renamed the  #ExcaliburIII) and made his 3,260-mile,  record-setting flight from Norway to Alaska.
Charles Blair bet the crew members that he  could make a flight from Norway to Alaska with just a $12 surplus astrocompass in a P-51 Mustang.
At the time, flying over the North Pole to  reach the U.S.S.R. was a difficult feat that required three navigators  and a thousand pounds of navigational equipment. Blair knew he could do  better.
One evening at a bar, Charles Blair made a  bet that would change the course of his career. 		He was hanging out with @USAirForce crew members who were involved in mapping the fastest route to  the Soviet Union during the #ColdWar.
Charles Blair was a pilot for commercial  airlines including United Airlines, Pan American Airlines, and American  Export Airlines. While working as a commercial pilot, Blair would become  bored on long flights and decided to teach himself celestial navigation  for the fun of it.
In 1952, Charles Blair published his  autobiography, "Red Ball in the Sky," and in 1956, he co-wrote "Thunder  Above" with A.J. Wallis, which was filmed as "Beyond the Curtain" in  1960.
In honor of #BookLoversDay, today we're highlighting @U.S. Air Force Brig. Gen. Charles F. Blair who was a pilot with the first squadron of jets to fly over #NorthPole May 29, 1951.		(Picture courtesy of the @Smithsonian @AirAndSpace Museum) https://t.co/gGlb9DRMlN

Matthew Henson is laid to rest in Section 8, Grave S-15-1.	   	#HonorThem
For more information about Matthew Henson, visit https://t.co/DoMJ4AMsJo.
Members of Matthew Henson's family attended the ceremony along with many of the explorer's admirers from around the world. The re-interment represented the ultimate national recognition that Henson had so long deserved.
On April 6, 1988, the remains of Matthew Henson and his wife were transported to Washington, D.C., where they were re-interred among other American heroes and near the grave site of Robert Peary and his wife Josephine Deibitsch Peary.
In 1987, at the request of Dr. S. Allen Counter of @Harvard University, President Ronald Reagan granted permission for Henson and his wife to be re-interred at #ArlingtonNationalCemetery.
After his death in 1955, Matthew Henson was buried in New York City's Woodlawn Cemetery. In 1968, the body of his wife Lucy Ross Henson was buried nearby.
Matthew Henson was three times refused a pension by Congress, was excluded from the Explorer's Club in which Peary was president, and lastly was not considered for burial at #ArlingtonNationalCemetery at the time of his death.
Matthew Henson, however, received comparatively little recognition for  his part in the discovery, even though Peary repeatedly acknowledged  Henson's indispensability to the success of the Polar expeditions.
During his lifetime, Robert Peary received many international honors for his achievement.
On that historic day, it was Matthew Henson, an African-American, who first reached the Pole and planted the U.S. flag.
On April 6, 1909, two U.S. citizens and four Inuit became the first human beings to set foot on the North Pole. The achievement crowned numerous attempts to reach the Pole, over a period of 18 years, by Robert Edwin Peary and Matthew Henson.

Information for this thread courtesy of the National Purple Heart Hall of Honor museum in New York. 		Learn more at https://t.co/bgM6Oc3URz.
This Saturday, join @MountVernon and @moph_hq at 10:30 a.m. as they pay tribute to nation's oldest military decoration. 		More information at https://t.co/3GUT6TxiNJ.
The #PurpleHeart, is awarded in the name of @POTUS to any member of the armed forces of the U.S. who, while serving under competent authority in any capacity with one of the U.S. armed services after April 5, 1917, has been wounded, killed or has died after being wounded.
Originally the #PurpleHeart was awarded for meritorious service. Being wounded was considered to be one type of merit. 		With the creation of the #LegionOfMerit in 1942, the award of the Purple Heart for meritorious service became unnecessary and was therefore discontinued.
Also in that year, the Purple Heart was made available for posthumous award to any member of the military killed on or after Dec. 7, 1941.
In 1942, President Franklin Roosevelt signed an executive order allowing the @USNavy to award the #PurpleHeart to Sailors, Marines and Coast Guard personnel.
The #PurpleHeart has undergone many changes with respect to the criteria for being awarded. At first, the Purple Heart was exclusively awarded to @USArmy and Army Air Corps personnel and could not be awarded posthumously to the next of kin.
On May 28, 1932, 137 #WorldWarI veterans were conferred their Purple Hearts at Temple Hill, in New Windsor, New York. Temple Hill was the site of the New Windsor Cantonment, which was the final encampment of the Continental Army in the winter of 1782-1783.
Gen. John J. "Black Jack" Pershing suggested a need for an award for merit in 1918, but it wasn't until 1932 that the #PurpleHeart was created in recognition of Gen. George Washington's ideals and for the bicentennial of his birth.
Gen. George Washington stated that the award was to be a permanent one, but once the #RevolutionaryWar ended, the Badge of Merit was all but forgotten until the 20th century.
At the present time there are three known recipients of the Badge of Military Merit: Sgt. Elijah Churchill, Sgt. William Brown, and Sgt. Daniel Bissel.
This award was open only to enlisted men and granted them the distinction of being permitted to pass all guards and sentinels as could commissioned-officers. The names of the recipients were to have been kept in a "Book of Merit."
The Continental Congress had forbidden Gen. George Washington from granting commissions and promotions in rank to recognize merit. 		Yet he wanted to honor merit, particularly among enlisted Soldiers. 		On Aug. 7, 1782, his general orders established the Badge of Military Merit.
The award known as the Purple Heart has a history that reaches back to the waning days of the #RevolutionaryWar.
#OnThisDay in 1782, former President and Gen. George Washington established the Badge of Military Merit, what is now known as the #PurpleHeart.		#PurpleHeartDay #HonorRememberExplore		(Image courtesy of @MountVernon) https://t.co/kkI9znEa6I

Brevet Maj. Pierre Charles L'Enfant is laid to rest in Section 2, Grave S-3, overlooking the capital city he designed.		#HonorThem
On the east end (the design facing the Arlington House) is a circle, 2 feet 7 inches in diameter, enclosing the plan of the City of Washington laid out by Brevet Maj. Pierre Charles L'Enfant.
The monument is made of white marble consisting of four slabs. The top slab is supported on six marble posts.
It was May 22, 1911, that the monument marking Brevet Maj. Pierre Charles L'Enfant's grave was dedicated. The service was conducted on the portico of the #ArlingtonHouse.
Many notables attended including the chief justice and justices of the #SupremeCourt, many senators and members of #Congress, high-ranking military, city officials, diplomatic corps and Washington socialites.
President William Howard Taft made the dedication address. He was followed by Ambassador Jusserand of France. The concluding address was made by Sen. Elihu Root. More than 350 people attended the ceremony.
Brevet Maj. Pierre Charles L'Enfant's remains were then taken by military escort to Arlington National Cemetery, and he was reinterred at 4 p.m. on the slope in front of the @ArlingtonNPS.
They remained there until April 28, 1909, where a military escort conveyed the remains to the @USCapitol where they lay in state from 9 a.m.-noon that day.
Brevet Maj. Pierre Charles L'Enfant's remains were placed in a metal-lined casket, covered with the U.S. flag and moved to Mount Olivet Cemetery on Bladensburg Road, Washington D.C.
In 1908 the Board of Commissioners of the City of Washington requested the Secretary of War to make available a suitable burial site at #ArlingtonNationalCemetery, and on April 22, 1909, the remains of Brevet Maj. Pierre Charles L'Enfant were disinterred from the Digges Farm.
He died June 14, 1825, and was interred on the Digges Farm, also known as Green Hill, in Prince Georges County, Maryland.
Under the direction of President George Washington, Brevet Maj. Pierre Charles L'Enfant planned the federal city of @WashingtonDC.

As tragic as the loss was, the @USNavy learned from its mistakes and implemented changes that improved reporting procedures, requirements for ship escorts, and increased lifesaving equipment.
A team of civilian researchers led by entrepreneur and philanthropist @PaulGAllen discovered the #USSIndianapolis' wreck Aug. 19, 2017.
Around 800 of the ship‚Äôs 1,196 Sailors and Marines survived the sinking, but after four to five days in the water -- suffering exposure, dehydration, drowning, and shark attacks -- only 316 survived.
Indianapolis was en route from Guam to Leyte when she was torpedoed and sunk by the Japanese submarine I-58 within the first hour of July 30.
The loss of #USSIndianapolis was a tragic moment following the completion of a secret mission that directly contributed to #WorldWarII‚Äôs end. After a successful high-speed run to deliver atomic bomb components to Tinian, the decorated Portland-class cruiser continued to Guam.
Due to communications and other errors, the #USSIndianapolis' loss went unnoticed until survivors were seen from a passing aircraft on Aug. 2, 1945. Four days earlier, she had delivered atomic bomb components used on Japan in August.

Some  information for this thread is courtesy of https://t.co/6G7pRUekrt.
The names of all the officers and enlisted men of the First Cavalry who lost their lives during the Spanish-American War are engraved on the imposing granite stone.
The monument is a large, dark-grey granite stone that displays the insignia of the First U.S. Volunteer Cavalry on its west face. It also lists the battles in which the #RoughRiders took part ‚Äî Las Guasimas, San Juan and Santiago.
The monument in Section 22 at Arlington National Cemetery was erected to  their memory by the members and friends of the regiment in 1906. The  official dedication took place April 12, 1907.
Many of the battles that earned the Rough Riders their famed distinction and accolades were also fought by #BuffaloSoldiers. A great roll-up of Buffalo Soldiers' contributions (and lack of recognition for those contributions) is available at https://t.co/UHSZlNh4LJ.
The #RoughRiders served with distinction during the #SpanishAmericanWar.
But mention the name 'Rough Riders,' and visions come to mind of the charge up San Juan Hill, led by Leonard Wood and Roosevelt.
The "Cowboy Cavalry" became one of the best-known cavalry units in U.S. history, though few people remember their official title, the First U.S. Volunteer Cavalry.
Theodore Roosevelt recruited a diverse group of cowboys, miners, law enforcement officials, and Native Americans to join the Rough Riders.
Before becoming president of the United States, Theodore Roosevelt was the Assistant Secretary of the @USNavy. He resigned in 1898 to organize the #RoughRiders in the #SpanishAmericanWar. The U.S. was fighting against Spain over Spain's colonial policies with Cuba.
Today is national #DayOfTheCowboy.		#DidYouKnow there is a monument at Arlington dedicated to the #RoughRiders, President Theodore Roosevelt's "Cowboy Cavalry?" Sgt. Cornelius Charlton is laid to rest in Section 40, Grave 300.		#HonorThem
Information for this thread courtesy of https://t.co/l8YpicQTrm.
Decades later, in 1990, Sgt. Cornelius Charlton's remains were reinterred at the American Legion Cemetery in Beckley, West Virginia, and was reinterred in 2008 to his final resting place here at Arlington National Cemetery.
It took many years for Sgt. Cornelius Charlton to get a proper burial. According to congressional records, his body was returned to the U.S. and buried in his mother's family plot.
Sgt. Cornelius Charlton also earned the #PurpleHeart, the Korean Service Medal and many other citations during his military career.
Sgt. Cornelius Charlton was one of only two black men to receive the award for actions performed during the #KoreanWar.
Sgt. Cornelius Charlton was credited with saving many of the Soldiers in his platoon. His courage, leadership and self-sacrifice earned him the #MedalOfHonor, which he posthumously received in early 1952.
Sgt. Cornelius Charlton went ahead of the rest of the Soldiers and raked the position with bullets, destroying two Chinese machine guns and forcing back its defenders. But he was hit by a second grenade during the charge - an injury that proved fatal.
The platoon then spotted the Chinese bunker that was firing the mortars from the far side of the hill, so Sgt. Cornelius Charlton again urged them forward to destroy it.
Sgt. Cornelius Charlton suffered a serious chest wound during that time, but he wouldn't give in. He refused medical attention and instead led a third charge up the hill, which took them to the crest of the ridge.
Using a rifle and grenades, Sgt. Cornelius Charlton managed to destroy two hostile positions and kill six enemy soldiers before his unit got pinned down on the hill they were attacking. He tried to push the men forward, but they were driven back again by heavy grenade fire.
His unit's leader was injured and had to be evacuated, so Charlton assumed command and rallied the troops to continue the assault.
On June 2, 1951, Sgt. Cornelius Charlton's platoon was attacking a hill that was heavily defended by Chinese infantrymen and mortars near the village of Chipo-ri, northeast of Seoul.
Charlton was awarded the #MedalOfHonor during #OperationPiledriver, a major battle in the #KoreanWar that was aimed at pushing North Korean and Chinese troops out of the south.
Sgt. Cornelius Charlton was initially assigned to an engineering unit, but he requested and received a transfer into Company C, an infantry unit that was part of the 24th Infantry Regiment.
Sgt. Cornelius Charlton was raised in southern West Virginia, but his family eventually moved to the Bronx, New York City. In 1946, he graduated from high school there and immediately enlisted into the @USArmy, just at the end of #WorldWarII.
Months before the 24th Inf. Regt. was deactivated, though, 21-year-old Sgt. Cornelius Charlton gave his last full measure of devotion to the country.
This came during the #KoreanWar, which also became the first war since the #AmericanRevolution to see black Soldiers fighting alongside white Soldiers in the same units.
Sgt. Cornelius Charlton was a member of the @USArmy's all-black 24th Infantry Regiment, @25ID, one of the last segregated units to engage in combat.
In honor of #BuffaloSoldiersDay, #TodayIRemember @USArmy Sgt. Cornelius Charlton, who was one of the last #BuffaloSoldiers.		Who are you remembering today?		#HonorThem		(Headstone photo courtesy of Arlington National Cemetery. Portrait courtesy of #USArmy.) https://t.co/SECmujR53H

For more information about the desegregation of the armed forces, visit https://t.co/4W5gmzQ3fd.
Although it was years before the services effectively integrated, national cemeteries throughout the country adopted the policy immediately and disbanded burial segregation regulation in 1948.
The new policy was to go into effect "as rapidly as possible, having due regard to the time required to effectuate any necessary changes without impairing efficiency or morale."
Executive Order 9981 stated "that there shall be equality of treatment and opportunity for all persons in the armed services without regard to race, color, religion, or national origin."
Arlington National Cemetery was established in 1864, and for more than 80 years African-Americans were buried separately from white service members.
#OnThisDay in 1948, President Harry S. Truman issued Executive Order 9981, which ended segregation in the @DeptofDefense.		#ThrowbackThursday #TBT #HonorRememberExplore 		(Newspaper cover courtesy of the @LibraryCongress) https://t.co/KJY4Ks1AiL

William J. Brennan is laid to rest with his first and second wives, Marjorie Leonard Brennan and Mary Fowler Brennan in Section 5, Grave 40-1.	   	#HonorThem
Justice David Souter, who succeeded William J. Brennan to the high court, said that 'One can agree with the Brennan opinions and one may disagree with them, but their collective influence is an enormously powerful defining force in the contemporary life of this republic.'
William J. Brennan received the Presidential Medal of Freedom in 1993.
In an essay published in 1997, William J. Brennan wrote, 'The genius of the Constitution rests not in any static meaning it may have had in a world that is dead and gone, but in the adaptability of its great principles to cope with current problems and present needs.'
After William J. Brennan himself retired following a debilitating stroke, he continued to espouse his judicial philosophy.
President Dwight D. Eisenhower gave William J. Brennan a recess appointment to the #SupremeCourt on Oct. 16, 1956, to succeed Justice Sherman Minton, who was retiring for health reasons. The Senate confirmed the appointment March 19, 1957, by voice vote.
After #WorldWarII, William J. Brennan returned to his practice, specializing in labor law, but took an appointment to a state court in 1949. Three years later the Republican governor appointed him to the New Jersey Supreme Court.
Attending law school on a scholarship, William J. Brennan completed his education and practiced law in Newark, New Jersey, until entering the @USArmy in 1942. He rose to the rank of colonel, handling labor disputes while serving on the staff of the secretary of war.
William J. Brennan was renowned for his persistence in building consensus, employing civility and persuasive reason to sway others to his view.
William J. Brennan's view of the role of the court led to decisions that expanded the rights of racial minorities and women, fostered protections for free speech, and led to reapportionment of voting districts along the 'one person, one vote' principle.
William J. Brennan was one of the most influential members of the #SupremeCourt, authoring more than 1,360 opinions during his 34 years on the Supreme Court.
#TodayIRemember @USArmy Col. and Associate Justice of the U.S. Supreme Court, William J. Brennan Jr., who died #OnThisDay in 1997.		Who are you remembering today?	  	#HonorThem		(Headstone photo by Rachel Larue, portrait courtesy of the @LibraryCongress) https://t.co/hyzy9hB4yF

Cmdr. Elliot McKay See is laid to rest in Section 4, Grave 208.		#HonorThem
The Gemini program was teaching #NASA what it needed to know to be able to send crews to the moon. Astronauts were learning to walk and work in zero gravity. Pilots were practicing rendezvous maneuvers and docking spacecraft.
Cmdr. Elliot McKay See and @USAirForce Maj. Charles Arthur Bassett II were killed in a plane crash at Lambert Field, St. Louis, Missouri, Feb. 28, 1966.
Cmdr. Elliot McKay See worked with the teams developing guidance and navigations systems and was named backup pilot for Gemini V, then command pilot for #Gemini IX.
Cmdr. Elliot McKay See was a flight engineer and test pilot for General Electric. He joined @NASA in September 1962, part of NASA's second astronaut class.

Gen. Roscoe Robinson earned two Silver Stars, three Legion of Merit awards, the Distinguished Flying Cross and a Bronze Star Medal.

Fleet Adm. William D. Leahy is laid to rest with his wife Louise in Section 2, Grave 932. 	   	#HonorThem
Information for this thread courtesy of https://t.co/NjAR6XFMeh.
The #USSLeahy was named in Fleet Adm. William D. Leahy's honor.
Though he retired in 1949, Fleet Adm. William D. Leahy technically remained on active duty until his death.
After Roosevelt's death in April 1945, Fleet Adm. William D. Leahy continued in his posts,  assisting President Harry S. Truman in bringing about final victory  against Germany and Japan, and then helping to guide the nation in the early post-war years.
In recognition of these accomplishments, Fleet Adm. William D. Leahy received promotion to the rank of fleet admiral in December 1944.
Upon his return home, Fleet Adm. William D. Leahy was recalled to @USNavy service as aide to President Franklin D. Roosevelt and as senior member of the Joint Chiefs of Staff. As such, he played a critical role in the strategy, diplomacy and execution of #WorldWarII.
Following retirement in August 1939, Fleet Adm. William D. Leahy was appointed Governor of Puerto Rico. In late 1940, he became the United States Ambassador to France, serving in that very demanding position until April 1942.
During this time Fleet Adm. William D. Leahy frequently had additional duty as Acting Secretary of the Navy.
In January 1937, Fleet Adm. William D. Leahy became chief of Naval Operations, serving for more than two years during a period marked by increasing tensions in the Far East and Europe and by a gradual expansion of the Navy.
In 1935 Fleet Adm. William D. Leahy became commander, Battleships, Battle Force, with the rank of vice admiral and then flew his four-star flag as commander, Battle Force.
After being promoted to rear admiral in 1927, Fleet Adm. William D. Leahy served as chief of the Bureau of Ordnance, was commander of the Scouting Force's destroyers and was chief of the Bureau of Navigation.
During the first half of the 1920s, Fleet Adm. William D. Leahy commanded the cruiser Saint Louis and battleship New Mexico.
In 1915-16, Fleet Adm. William D. Leahy was commanding officer of the gunboat Dolphin in the Caribbean area. He was next the executive officer of battleship Nevada, and, during #WorldWarI, commanded the transport Princess Matoika.
In the decade following Fleet Adm. William D. Leahy's return to the United States in 1904, he served in the cruisers Boston and California, was an instructor at the Naval Academy and chief of staff to the commander of U.S. forces in Nicaragua.
In 1899, Fleet Adm. William D. Leahy was sent to the Asiatic Station, where he participated in the suppression of the #PhilippineInsurrection and of the #BoxerRebellion in China.
Fleet Adm. William D. Leahy was born in Hampton, Iowa, May 6, 1875. He graduated from the U.S. @NavalAcademy in 1897 and was assigned to the battleship Oregon, serving in her through the #SpanishAmericanWar.
#OnThisDay in 1959, @USNavy Fleet Adm. William Daniel Leahy died.		(Photo of Leahy as a cadet courtesy of @USNHistory) https://t.co/vB7mjTCRnF
