Potter Stewart is laid to rest in Section 5, Grave 40-2.	   	#HonorThem
On July 19, 1983, Potter Stewart was appointed to the National Bipartisan Commission on Central America, and on July 28, 1983, he was appointed to the President's Commission on Organized Crime. He continued to be active until his death at the age of 70 in 1985.
Potter Stewart was nominated to the Supreme Court of the United States by President Dwight Eisenhower in October 1958, to fill the seat previously occupied by Justice Harold H. Burton. He retired July 3, 1981, but continued to sit by designation on various Courts of Appeals.
Potter Stewart then served as a member of the Cincinnati City Council from 1950-1953 and as vice-mayor between 1952-1953. From 1954-1958 Stewart served as judge on the U.S. Court of Appeals for the 9th Circuit, having been appointed by President Dwight Eisenhower.
Potter Stewart was admitted to the Ohio Bar in 1941 and the New York Bar in 1942. He left the law firm he was working at to join the @USNavy during #WorldWarII. In 1945, he returned to practice law, first in New York City, and then to his hometown of Cincinnati, Ohio.

Maj. Gen. William Donovan is laid to rest in Section 2, Grave 4874-RH.		#HonorThem
For more information on Maj. Gen. William Donovan, visit https://t.co/QuRC03dx61.
Maj. Gen. William Donovan was the first person in U.S. history to have earned the country‚Äôs four highest awards: The Congressional Medal of Honor, the Distinguished Service Cross, the Distinguished Service Medal, and the National Security Medal.
Maj. Gen. William Donovan is considered the father of American centralized intelligence. From 1942-1945, he led the Office of Strategic Services, which was the forerunner of today‚Äôs @CIA.
By the end of the war, Maj. Gen. William Donovan was one of the most decorated Soldiers of #WWI. Upon returning from Europe after the war, Donovan ‚Äî along with Theodore Roosevelt, Jr. ‚Äî was a co-founder of the @AmericanLegion.
#OnThisDay in 1918, @USArmy Maj. Gen. William "Wild Bill" Donovan was awarded the #MedalOfHonor for bravery under fire on the River Ourcq during the Second Battle of the Marne during #WorldWarI.		(Picture courtesy of the @CIA) https://t.co/jc5B7OtnSE

Gen. John J. Pershing is laid to rest in Section 34, Grave S-19-LH.		#HonorThem
Gen. John J. Pershing was regarded as a mentor by the generation of American generals who led the U.S. Army in Europe during #WorldWarII, including George C. Marshall, Dwight D. Eisenhower, Omar N. Bradley, and George S. Patton.

Col. Robert F. Overmyer is laid to rest in Section 23, Grave 22469.		#HonorThem
After serving on the inquiry panel following the Challenger disaster, Col. Robert F. Overmyer retired from the military and died in the crash of an experimental civilian aircraft he was test piloting. He was 59.
Col. Robert F. Overmyer was an astronaut for @NASA, and piloted STS-5, the first fully operational flight of the space shuttle, Nov. 11, 1982, when the Payload Assist Module was first used, launching two commercial satellites. He commanded STS 51-B, a Spacelab mission, in 1985.

Machinist's Mate 1st Class Robert R. Scott is laid to rest in Section 34, Grave 3939.	   	#HonorThem
Though the rest of the sailors evacuated the compartment, Machinist's Mate 1st Class Robert R. Scott refused to leave, saying words to the effect 'this is my station, and I will stay and give them air as long as the guns are going.'
As the compartment filled with water, and the ship slowly sank in #PearlHarbor, Machinist's Mate 1st Class Robert R. Scott continued to operate the compressor to provide air for crew members trapped below.
During the Dec. 7, 1941, Japanese air raid on #PearlHarbor, Machinist's Mate 1st Class Robert R. Scott was serving on board #USSCalifornia. 		The compartment to which Scott was assigned as a battle station was flooded after a torpedo hit.
Machinist's Mate 1st Class Robert R. Scott was born in Massillon, Ohio, and enlisted in the @USNavy during the 1930s.
Machinist's Mate 1st Class Robert R. Scott was one of the first service members to earn the #MedalOfHonor during #WorldWarII.
#TodayIRemember @USNavy Machinist's Mate 1st Class Robert R. Scott, who was born #OnThisDay in 1915. 		Who are you remembering today?		#HonorThem		(Headstone photo courtesy of Arlington National Cemetery; portrait courtesy of @USNHistory) https://t.co/xF33IO0RYY

#DIdYouKnow that from 1799 until 1921, the Marine Corps celebrated its birthday, or #MarineCorpsDay, on July 11? 		It was in 1921 when the date was permanently changed to Nov. 10 to commemorate the establishment of the Corps to aid in the #RevolutionaryWar.
After success in many campaigns, the Corps was abolished at the close of the #RevolutionaryWar for reasons of economy.
The #MarineCorps was established on Nov. 10, 1775, to augment naval forces in the #RevolutionaryWar. 		The recruiting headquarters was set up by Capt. Samuel Nicholas in the Tun Tavern on Water Street in Philadelphia, which is considered to be the birthplace of the #Marines.
#OnThisDay in 1798, Congress ordered the creation of the @USMC, named it the United States Marine Corps and directed that it be available for service under the Secretary of the @USNavy.		#HonorRememberExplore		(@USArmy photo of the #MarineCorps War Memorial by Elizabeth Fraser) https://t.co/i9D2UAOqZ8

Earl Warren is laid to rest in Section 21, Grave S-32.		#HonorThem
In 1953, President Dwight D. Eisenhower named Earl Warren as chief justice of the United States. He served as chief justice for 16 years and retired in 1969.
In 1948, Earl Warren ran on the Republican ticket as Thomas E. Dewey's vice presidential candidate, and in 1952, he sought the presidential nomination himself.
Earl Warren became governor of California in 1942, and was twice re-elected. 		After his term as governor, Warren made two attempts to gain a national political office.
#TodayIRemember former Chief Justice of the United States Earl Warren, who died #OnThisDay in 1974 at the age of 83. For more information about Ignacy Jan Paderewski, visit https://t.co/Ty8iyB780u.		#HonorRememberExplore
It was #OnThisDay in 1941 that Ignacy Jan Paderewski was temporarily interred in the vault of the Mast of the #USSMaine Memorial. 		It wasn't until 51 years later on June 26, 1992, that Paderewski's remains were returned to Poland.
Ignacy Jan Paderewski was not eligible for below-ground interment in #ArlingtonNationalCemetery or in any other national cemetery because he never served in the armed forces of the United States or any nations allied with the United States in #WorldWarI or #WorldWarII.
President Franklin D. Roosevelt said, 'He may lie there until Poland is free.'		#WorldWarII
Upon receiving word of Ignacy Jan Paderewski's death, June 29, 1941, President Franklin D. Roosevelt called the @StateDept and asked that they inform Paderewski's family and officials of the @PolishEmbassyUS that his body could be given a temporary resting place at the cemetery.
#ThrowbackThursday: Polish pianist, composer and statesman Ignacy Jan Paderewski on outdoor stage between 1915-1920.		#DidYouKnow Paderewski was temporarily interred in the #USSMaine  Memorial for more than 50 years?		(Photo courtesy of @LibraryCongress) https://t.co/SSNKPj228I

Col. Norman Pershing Hays is laid to rest in Section 4, Grave 5674-4.		#HonorThem
The Distinguished Flying Cross is awarded to service members who have distinguished his or herself in combat in support of operations by heroism or extraordinary achievement while participating in an aerial flight.
Col. Norman Pershing Hays served during #WorldWarII, and during his service, earned the Distinguished Flying Cross with Oak Leaf Cluster, indicating he earned the award twice.

Thurgood Marshall is laid to rest in Section 5, Grave 40-3.		#HonorThem
Thurgood Marshall said at a 1991 news conference on his retirement that he wished to be remembered with 10 words: 		'That he did what he could with what he had.'
Thurgood Marshall won all but three of the 32 cases he argued before the Supreme Court. One of his most famous landmark cases he won was the 1954 Brown ruling ending 'separate but equal' school systems.
Thurgood Marshall was the first African-American appointed to the Supreme Court, and during his 24-year tenure, he was the only black justice.
#TodayIRemember Thurgood Marshall, associate justice of the U.S. Supreme Court, who was born #OnThisDay in 1908.		Who are you remembering today?		#HonorThem		(Headstone photo courtesy of Arlington National Cemetery; portrait courtesy of the @LibraryCongress) https://t.co/meC4WEZIxg

Lt. Col. Juanita Redmond Hipps is laid to rest in Section 21, Grave 769-1.		#HonorThem
Lt. Col. Juanita Redmond Hipps was one of the few nurses to escape and wrote "I Served on Bataan," a best-seller in 1943, which later became the basis for the movie "So Proudly We Hail," a story about military nurses sent to the Philippines during #WWII.
Lt. Col. Juanita Redmond Hipps was one of the Angels of Bataan, and served as a nurse during #WorldWarII in Bataan and Corregidor. When Bataan and Corregidor fell, almost 80 nurses were captured and imprisoned.

The United States' heroes are buried here from every U.S. conflict, from the #RevolutionaryWar to the conflicts of the 21st century.
What was once created out of necessity during the #CivilWar, #ArlingtonNationalCemetery has become a national shrine for hundreds of thousands active duty service members, veterans and their families who have served during times of war and times of peace.
Our historic cemetery bears witness to our heritage and the service and sacrifice of men and women in uniform throughout our nation‚Äôs history.
@ArlingtonNPS' #ArlingtonHouse stands on the hillside rising above the #PotomacRiver and overlooking @WashingtonDC. The 19th-century mansion is surrounded by more than 260,000 military gravesites.

Capt. Burton K. McCord is laid to rest in Section 3, Grave 4617-A.		#HonorThem
Additional information for this thread courtesy of https://t.co/JZWeZSMHtQ.
U.S. operations in Vietnam relied upon American superiority in technology and manpower which, against an insurgent and subsistence force, ultimately was of no avail. 		The U.S. Military Assistance Command, Vietnam, was abolished March 29, 1973.
Its commander, the senior U.S. military commander in Vietnam, had direct responsibility for all U.S. military policy, operations, and assistance in Vietnam, with authority to discuss these issues with the President and other officials of the government of Vietnam.
The U.S. Military Assistance Command, Vietnam, was established in Saigon, Feb. 8, 1962, and was a subordinate command of @PacificCommand.
During his service, Capt. Burton K. McCord earned a #SilverStar with Oak Leaf Cluster (indicating he earned the Silver Star twice) and the #BronzeStar.
Capt. Burton K. McCord graduated from @WestPoint_USMA in 1963, and served in the U.S. Military Assistance Command, Vietnam, during the #VietnamWar.
#TodayIRemember @USArmy Capt. Burton K. McCord, who was born #OnThisDay in 1941.		Who are you remembering today?		#HonorThem		(Headstone photo courtesy of Arlington National Cemetery. Other photos and information submitted by McCord‚Äôs classmates, family and friends.) https://t.co/Sikqw3njOf

Learn more about the #KoreanWar directly from its veterans at https://t.co/ogeF2fJfKG.
The pine tree was replaced by President Roh Tae Woo on Oct. 17, 1989. A Korean Mountain Ash was planted behind the bench on June 15, 1989.
The #KoreanWar Contemplative Bench is located on the north side of the #MemorialAmphitheater beside a Korean White Pine tree that was donated by President Kim of Korea in 1965.
The #KoreanWar lasted just over three years, but more than 65 years after the guns fell silent, U.S. men and women in uniform still maintain the peace along the 38th Parallel, the border between North and South.
More than 5.7 million U.S. service members served in the #KoreanWar on the battle lines, in the air, or in support of those whose lives were at risk.
#OnThisDay 68 years ago, war broke out on the Korean peninsula as forces from the communist North invaded the South.		#DidYouKnow there is a #KoreanWar Contemplative Bench on the grounds?		#HonorRememberExplore		(@USArmy file photo by Arlington National Cemetery) https://t.co/AvjauwNCRY
Gen. Hap Arnold is laid to rest in Section 34, Grave 44-A.		#HonorThem
Information for this thread courtesy of https://t.co/5Lj9DxrueM.
On May 7, 1949 Gen. Hap Arnold was appointed the first general of the Air Force, five-star rank, by Congress. He died at his ranch home, Valley of the Moon, near Sonoma, California, Jan. 15, 1950. 		His name is perpetuated at the @AEDCnews at Arnold Air Force Base, Tennessee.
During his long career, Gen. Hap Arnold wrote a number of books, including early boys' books to create interest among youth in flying, and the post-#WorldWarII autobiography "Global Mission," an accurate account of @USAirForce activities in the war and his own life.
Gen. Hap Arnold retired from the service June 30, 1946, after earning three Distinguished Service crosses, the Distinguished Flying  Cross, Air Medal and decorations from Morocco, Brazil, Yugoslavia, Peru,  France, Mexico and Great Britain.
In March 1943 Gen. Hap Arnold was promoted to four-star general. He suffered a heart  attack in 1945 as the war drew to a close, attributed by his doctors to overwork.
Early in 1943 Gen. Hap Arnold made a 35,000-mile tour of North Africa, Middle East, India and China, and attended the Casablanca Conferences.
Prior to and all during #WorldWarII, he directed air activities for the nation's global war against Germany and Japan. Under him the air arm grew from 22,000 officers and men with 3,900 planes to nearly 2,500,000 men and 75,000 aircraft.
Gen. Hap Arnold 's title was changed to chief of the Army Air Forces on June 30, 1941, and that December he got a third star. When the War Department General Staff was organized in March 1942 Arnold became commanding general of U.S. Army Air Forces.
In January 1936 he became assistant to the chief of Air Corps in Washington and on Sept. 29, 1938 was promoted to major general and appointed chief of Air Corps.
Gen. Hap Arnold was gaining a reputation as a bomber man, having encouraged development of the B-17 Flying Fortress and B-24 Liberator four-engine planes, and the precision training of crewmembers.
In February 1935 Gen. Hap Arnold was jumped two grades to brigadier general and put in command of the 1st Wing of General Headquarters Air Force at March Field, California.
In July and August 1934 Gen. Hap Arnold personally organized and led a flight of 10  Martin B-10 bombers in a round-trip record flight from Washington, D.C.,  to Fairbanks, Alaska, and next year received his second Mackay Trophy for this achievement.
Gen. Hap Arnold was promoted to lieutenant colonel in February 1931 as commanding officer of March Field, California.
In June 1929 Gen. Hap Arnold completed the Command and General Staff School at @FortLeavenworth and was assigned as commanding officer of the air depot at Fairfield, Ohio.
Gen. Hap Arnold devotedly shared Mitchell's beliefs in the strategic capability of the airplane and urged an independent air arm which Arnold lived to see authorized in 1947. 		He next went to @FortRiley, Kansas, where he commanded Air Corps troops at Marshall Field until 1928.
Gen. Hap Arnold testified on behalf of Gen. Billy Mitchell during the famed courts-martial in the fall of 1925 when Mitchell was found guilty of insubordination.
Gen. Hap Arnold graduated from the Army Industrial College in 1925 and became chief of the Information Division in the Office of the Chief of Air Corps.
In June 1920 Gen. Hap Arnold went back to captain's grade, but next month was promoted to major, where he remained until 1931. In October 1922 he became commanding officer of Rockwell Field, California, serving two years.
Gen. Hap Arnold went to France in November 1918 at war's end on an inspection tour of aviation activities. He returned in January 1919 as supervisor of the Air Service at Coronado, California, and as air officer of the 9th Corps Area at the Presidio at San Francisco.
Gen. Hap Arnold was in charge of Information Service in the Aviation Division of the Signal Corps. When the Office of Military Aeronautics was created, Arnold became assistant executive officer and in February 1918 was named assistant director.
In February 1917 Gen. Hap Arnold went to Panama to organize an air service there, which he commanded until May 1917. As the U.S. entered #WorldWarI, he was called back to Washington, promoted to major June 17, 1917, and on Aug. 5 was promoted to full colonel.
Gen. Hap Arnold stayed there until March 1916 and came home for two months at Madison  Barracks, New York, before going back to aviation duties in May 1916,  with promotion to captain and duty at the new flying school at San  Diego, California.
In September 1913 Gen. Hap Arnold went back to the 13th Infantry.
In November 1912 Gen. Hap Arnold was assigned to the Office of the Chief Signal Officer in Washington, where he was promoted to first lieutenant in April 1913.
During the latter part of 1912 Gen. Hap Arnold went to @FortRiley, Kansas, as an aerial observer of Field Artillery firing and was the first military aviator to use radio to report his observations.
Gen. Hap Arnold flew the early type of Wright biplane with its 40 horsepower engine revolving two propellers by the chain-and-sprocket method.
On Oct. 9, 1912, Gen. Hap Arnold won the first MacKay Trophy ever awarded for a reconnaissance flight on a triangular course from College Park to Washington Barracks, D.C. to Fort Myer, Virginia, and return to College Park.
On June 1, 1912, Gen. Hap Arnold established a new altitude record by piloting a Burgess-Wright airplane to a height of 6,540 feet. He also took part in air maneuvers in New York and Connecticut and set several records.
Gen. Hap Arnold then was assigned to teach other flyers at the Signal Corps aviation school at College Park, Maryland. The school was moved to Augusta, Georgia, November, and he served there until April 1912 when he went back to College Park for flight duty.
The Wright brothers, who had made their first flight in 1903, personally instructed Gen. Hap Arnold for two months, after which he soloed and became one of the earliest military aviators in June 1911.
Gen. Hap Arnold returned home for two-and-a-half-years' duty at Governors Island, New  York, until April 1911 when he was detailed to the Signal Corps and  sent to Dayton, Ohio, for instructions in the Wright biplane.
Gen. Hap Arnold initially was assigned to the 29th Infantry, serving with it in the Philippine Islands for two years.
Three years later, by act of #Congress, Gen. Hap Arnold received permanent five-star rank as general of the @USAirForce, the first such commission ever granted.
From then on Gen. Hap Arnold's life paralleled the growth of America's air power and he personally contributed to most of the major milestones of development during the long period until he retired in 1946.
"Hap" Arnold, as he was fondly known and called, dating from his early days at #WestPoint, was in the class of 1907 at @WestPoint_USMA.

#DidYouKnow when the United States entered #WorldWarII, 50,000 @USCoastGuardAux members joined the war effort as military teams? 		Many of their private vessels were placed into service in an effort to protect the U.S.
The @USCoastGuardAux has units in all 50 states, Puerto Rico, the Virgin Islands, American Samoa, and Guam.
Passage of this act designated the Reserve as a military branch of the active service, while the civilian section, formerly referred to as the Coast Guard Reserve, became the Auxiliary under title 14, chapter 23 of the USC.
Two years later, on Feb. 19, Congress amended the 1939 act with passage of the Auxiliary and Reserve Act of 1941.
When the Coast Guard "Reserve" was authorized by act of Congress on #OnThisDay in 1939, the @USCG was given a legislative mandate to use civilian to promote safety on and over the high seas and the nation's navigable waters.

Lt. Col. Willard Bennett died Sept. 28, 1987. He is laid to rest in the Columbarium in Court 2, Section O, Column 2, Niche 2.		#HonorThem
Information for this thread courtesy of https://t.co/6fh180gLMU.
The radio frequency mass spectrometer was developed in 1950. It was first launched into space on the #SputnikIII to measure the masses of atoms. 		It was the only space instrument used by the Russians and credited to a U.S. inventor in their own Russian-language publications.
It also reproduced intricate impact patterns found on the earth's surface which explained many features of the polar aurora.
In the 1950s, Lt. Col. Willard Bennett's experimental tube called the Stormertron predicted and modeled the Van Allen radiation belts surrounding the earth six years before they were discovered by satellite.
Lt. Col. Willard Bennett made scientific history in the 1930s pioneering studies in plasma physics. These studies have been used throughout the world in controlled thermonuclear fusion research.
Following service in #WorldWarII, Lt. Col. Willard Bennett worked at the National Bureau of Standards (now the @usnistgov), the @UArkansas, and the @USNRL. 		In 1961, he was appointed Burlington Professor of Physics at @ncstate (emeritus in 1976).
Born in Findlay, Ohio, Lt. Col. Willard Bennett attended @CarnegieMellon's Institute of Technology and @OhioState University. 		He received his master's from the @UWMadison in 1926 and his Ph.D. from the @UMich in 1928. 		In 1930 he joined the Physics faculty at Ohio State.
Lt. Col. Willard Bennett invented the Radio Frequency Mass Spectrometer and was issued U.S. Patent number 2,721,271.
In honor of @USPTO recently issuing patent number 10 million, today we are highlighting @USArmy Lt. Col. Willard Bennett. 		#10MillionPatents #HonorThem		(Photo courtesy of the @InventorsHOF) https://t.co/O0pOW0CErA

Maj. Audie L. Murphy is laid to rest in Section 46, Grave 366-11.	   	#HonorThem	   	(@USArmy photo by Rachel Larue, Arlington National Cemetery) https://t.co/RK6F4nA6jH
Information for this thread courtesy of https://t.co/miQcH24UcY.
At the end of a row of graves, his tomb is marked by a simple, white, government-issue tombstone, which lists only a few of his many military decorations. 		The stone is, as he was, too small.
Audie Murphy is buried in Arlington National Cemetery, just across Memorial Drive from the #MemorialAmphitheater. A special flagstone walkway has been constructed to accommodate the large number of people who stop to pay their respects to this hero.
Murphy, who once said that he could only sleep with a loaded pistol under his pillow, was haunted by nightmares of his wartime experiences throughout his adult life. In 1971, at the age of 46, he died in the crash of a private plane near Roanoke, Virginia.
After nearly 20 years he retired from acting and started a career in private business. But the venture was unsuccessful, eventually forcing him into bankruptcy in 1968.
Many film critics, however, believe his best performance was in Red Badge of Courage, Stephen Crane's #CivilWar epic.
Nevertheless, he went on to make more than 40 films. His first part was just a small one in Beyond Glory in 1948. 		The following year he published his wartime memoirs, To Hell and Back, which received good reviews. Later he portrayed himself in the 1955 movie version.
Still very shy and unassuming, Murphy arrived in Hollywood with only his good looks and ‚Äî by his own account ‚Äî "no talent."
Audie Murphy returned to a hero's welcome in the United States. 		His photograph appeared on the cover of @Life magazine and he was persuaded by actor James Cagney to embark on an acting career.
Murphy had been wounded three times during the war, yet, in May 1945, when victory was declared in Europe, he had still not reached his 21st birthday.
By the war's end, Murphy had become the nation's most-decorated soldier, earning an unparalleled 28 medals, including three from France and one from Belgium.
His courageous performance stalled the German advance and allowed him to lead his men in the counterattack which ultimately drove the enemy from Holtzwihr. For this Murphy was awarded the #MedalOfHonor, the nation's highest award for gallantry in action.
Wounded in the leg during the heavy fire, Murphy remained there for  nearly an hour, repelling the attack of German soldiers on three sides  and single-handedly killing 50 of them.
Alone, he mounted an abandoned burning tank destroyer and, with a single machine gun, contested the enemy's advance.
On Jan. 26, 1945, near the village of Holtzwihr in eastern France, Murphy's forward positions came under fierce attack by the Germans. 		Against the onslaught of six Panzer tanks and 250 infantrymen, Murphy ordered his men to fall back to better their defenses.
But his biggest test was yet to come.
Shortly thereafter his unit was withdrawn to train for Operation Anvil-Dragoon, the invasion of southern France. During 7 weeks of fighting in that successful campaign, Murphy's division suffered 4,500 casualties, and he became one of the most decorated men in his company.
Throughout these campaigns, Murphy's skills earned him advancements in  rank, because many of his superior officers were being transferred, wounded or killed.		After the capture of Rome, Murphy earned his first decoration for gallantry.
He landed at Salerno to fight in the Voltuno river campaign and then at Anzio to be part of the Allied force that fought its way to Rome.
It was there in 1943 that he first saw combat, proving himself to be a  proficient marksman and highly skilled soldier, consistently his performance demonstrated how well he understood the techniques of small-unit action.
Following basic training Murphy was assigned to the 15th Regiment, @3rd_Infantry Division in North Africa preparing to invade Sicily. 		#RockOfTheMarne
Not tall enough to meet the minimum requirements, he tried to enlist in the paratroopers, but again was denied entrance. 		Despondent, he chose the infantry.
War had just been declared and, like so many other young men, Murphy lied about his age in his attempt to enlist. 		But it was not his age that kept him out of the Marines; it was his size.
Left at a very young age to help raise 10 brothers and sisters when his father deserted their mother, Audie was 16 when his mother died. He watched as his siblings were doled out to an orphanage or relatives. 		Seeking an escape from that life in 1942, he looked to the #Marines.
He was Audie Murphy, the baby-faced Texas farmboy who was born #OnThisDay in 1924. He became a U.S. legend. 		Murphy grew up on a sharecropper's farm in Hunt County, Texas.
He wanted to join the @USMC, but he was too short. 		The paratroopers wouldn't have him either. 		Reluctantly, he settled on the infantry, enlisting to become nothing less than one of the most-decorated heroes of #WorldWarII.	   	(#USArmy photo) https://t.co/SOyUdeIbxB

Learn more about African-American history at Arlington National Cemetery at https://t.co/EQzUOBuMDr.		#HonorRememberExplore
#OnThisDay in 1865, @USArmy Major Gen. Gordon Granger led Union soldiers into Galveston, Texas, with news that the #CivilWar had ended and slavery was abolished - two years after the #EmancipationProclamation.
#Juneteenth is the oldest nationally celebrated commemoration of the ending of slavery in the United States.
Existing for more than 30 years, Freedman's Village provided housing, education, training for employment, medical care and food for former slaves.		(Image courtesy of @ArlingtonVALib) https://t.co/0oPYZt2Bz1
The village was run by the Freedmen's Bureau during most of its existence, and at one point employed U.S. Colored Troops (African-Americans who served in segregated @USArmy regiments during the war) to protect fugitive slaves from their former slave owners.
Arlington Estate was selected as the site because of its location outside of Washington D.C. where camps were overcrowded and in poor condition. More than 1,100 freed slaves lived at Freedman's Village where they farmed and lived during and after the #CivilWar.
"Contrabands" was used by the U.S. government to designate slaves who were freed as Union forces moved South, or who had escaped from local Virginia and Maryland slave owners.
In honor of #Juneteenth, today we are highlighting Freedman's Village, which was established on the southeast portion of the Arlington Estate in June 1863, as a camp for #CivilWar "Contrabands."		#HonorRememberExplore		(Map courtesy of Arlington National Cemetery) https://t.co/fBxYYr7ljn
The @USArmy Rangers' history predates the Revolutionary War, but June  19, 1942, is when then-Maj. William O. Darby organized and activated the  1st Ranger Battalion at Carrickfergus, Northern Ireland. 		For more information on U.S. Army Rangers, visit https://t.co/iY1RABCndf.

A native of Colorado, Capt. Russell B. Rippetoe, was commissioned in the @USArmy as a field artillery officer Aug. 9, 1999.
Capt. Russell B. Rippetoe was the first Soldier killed in #OperationIraqiFreedom to be buried at Arlington National Cemetery.

Brig. Gen. Montgomery C. Meigs himself was later buried within 100 yards of Arlington House with his wife, father and son; the final statement to his original order.
A stone and masonry burial vault in the rose garden, 20 feet wide and 10 feet deep, and containing the remains of 1,800 #BullRun casualties, was among the first monuments to Union dead erected under Brig. Gen. Montgomery C. Meigs' orders.
Upon the authority of Secretary of War Edwin Stanton, Brig. Gen. Montgomery C. Meigs, Quartermaster General of the U.S. Army, appropriated the grounds for use as a military cemetery. At the time, he commanded the garrison at #ArlingtonHouse.

#DidYouKnow the U.S. military developed its own superior code machine - SIGABA - before entering the war?		No one was able to break that one.
While controversial for its secrecy, the decoding process has been widely credited with saving hundreds of thousands of lives and shortening the war by nearly two years.
Breaking Germany's codes, and later those of the Japanese, proved to be a huge advantage for the Allies.
On #DDay itself, it also helped Allied commanders get word of their troops' progress quicker than through their own communication channels.
Officials said the German codes intercepted before #DDay precisely pinpointed nearly all of the German fighting units in the Normandy area.
Instead of telling the world about it, though, leaders thought the device would be more useful if kept secret. So for years, German plans were hindered by the decrypted messages, including on #DDay.
Early in the war, a team of Polish and British experts - led by Alan Turing, whose life and work are depicted in the Oscar-winning movie "The Imitation Game" - cracked that code through what became the foundation for the modern computer.
The Germans had Enigma, which was thought to be unbreakable - until it wasn't.
Long story short, since radio was the standard communication of the time, the Allies and the Axis powers both needed machines to turn military plans into secret codes.
Decoding the great German code machine known as Enigma, then keeping that decoding device a secret, is one of the most brilliant strategies that came out of #WorldWarII.
5 - Decoding "Enigma" helped us win. 		An Enigma decryption machine, called a "bombe." This machine, made by National Cash Register of Dayton, Ohio, eliminated all possible encryptions from intercepted messages until it arrived at the correct solution. 		(@USAirForce photo https://t.co/cNVEBESFmF
Somehow, though, both sectors of U.S. troops managed to advance their positions for overall success.
Rough surf caused huge problems for the amphibious tanks launched at sea; only two of 29 made it to shore, while many of the infantrymen who stormed off the boats were gunned down by Germans. Gen. Omar Bradley, who led the Omaha forces, nearly considered abandoning the operation.
The Omaha offensive turned out to be the bloodiest of the day, largely in part because Army intelligence underestimated the German stronghold there.
Many also missed their landing spots, as did the seaborne forces, which landed more than a mile from their intended destination, thanks to strong currents.
Thousands of U.S. paratroopers were injured or killed during their drop behind enemy lines at Utah Beach, having been shot out of the sky by enemy fire or weighed down and drowned in flooded marshlands.
4 - #DDay was almost a tactical failure. 		While the ultimate goal of liberating France and ousting the Germans did happen, a lot went wrong on D-Day - especially for the U.S., who were the first to launch the invasion.
Three other beach invasions by Allied troops happened simultaneously: Great Britain and some smaller forces stormed Gold and Sword beaches, while the Canadians took Juno Beach.
Stories of how U.S. troops stormed the beaches of Normandy are legendary, with the names Omaha Beach and Utah Beach standing out in people's minds. 		But the invasion stretched out over 50 miles of land, so we couldn't do it alone.
3 - We only stormed two of the five beaches. 		Members of a U.S. landing party lend helping hands to other service members whose landing craft was sunk by enemy action off the coast of Normandy. 		(File photo) https://t.co/95EUmHrgtb
Only nine days in May and June seemed to fit those requirements, so commanders eventually settled on June 5; however, thanks to forecasts that showed a short window of good weather that day, Gen. Eisenhower decided last-minute to switch #DDay to the early hours of June 6.
H-Hour was also crucial in that it relied on those tides to be rising at that time. There also had to be an hour of daylight just beforehand for bombardment accuracy.
The day needed to be long for maximum air power usage; a near-full moon was needed to help guide ships and airborne troops; and the tides had to be strong enough to expose beach obstacles at low tide and float supply-filled landing vehicles far onto the beach during high tide.
A lot of weather-related requirements were necessary to pull #DDay off.
2 - #DDay was initially set for one day earlier. 		(Map courtesy of @USArmy Transportation Museum) https://t.co/giis177btO
He said there were actually several other D-Days during the war - Normandy was just the biggest and most well-known.
"Be advised that any amphibious operation has a 'departed date;' therefore the shortened term 'D-Day' is used."
While the true meaning remains up for debate, we'll go with what @USArmy Gen. Dwight D. Eisenhower said about it through his executive assistant, Brig. Gen. Robert Schultz:
Many experts have varying opinions, including that the D simply stood for "day," a code used for any important military operation. Others have said it's just alliteration, like "H-Hour," when a military assault begins.
Soldiers of the Army's 16th Infantry Regiment, wounded while storming Omaha Beach, wait by the chalk cliffs for evacuation to a field hospital for treatment after #DDay. 		(@USArmyCMH photo) https://t.co/14rCIcfrfT

The procession stopped once during the drive to Arlington National Cemetery at the #LincolnMemorial where the @MarineBand played "The Battle Hymn of the Republic." 		The funeral motorcade arrived at #ArlingtonNationalCemetery at 10:30 p.m.

The monument was the first memorial at Arlington to be dedicated to Soldiers who had died in battle, and who later could not be identified. It is presumed the vault contains the remains of both Confederate and Union Soldiers.
The #CivilWar Monument, dedicated September 1866, stands atop a masonry vault containing the remains of 2,111 soldiers gathered from the fields of #BullRun and the route to the Rappahannock. The remains were found scattered across the battlefields or in trenches and brought here.

Every year in May, hundreds of motorcyclists ride in one of three routes that cross the country, ending in the nation's capital and stopping at  war memorials, veterans' hospitals and schools on the way.
They talked to local radio, TV and newspapers about the thousands of men and women still unaccounted for from all wars.
.@RunForTheWall is an annual ride that honors those who did not return home from wars. It was started in 1989 by James Gregory and Bill Evans, two #VietnamWar veterans who traveled across the heartland of the United States on motorcycles.

These #MemorialDay ceremonies, rooted in 150 years of tradition, ensure that the United States will never forget those who died in the armed forces and  the country for which they served.	   	  #HonorThem
Every year, during #MemorialDay weekend, cemeteries across the globe honor service members who perished in the line of duty.
For many years, #MemorialDay commemorations both domestically and overseas took place on May 30. 		In 1968 Congress passed, and President Lyndon Johnson signed, the Uniform Monday Holiday Act. This legislation established the observance of Memorial Day on the last Monday in May.
As the meaning of #DecorationDay evolved, so did the name. By the late 19th century, many Americans were using the term #MemorialDay.
Over the years, the meaning of #DecorationDay evolved, gradually expanding from a commemoration of #CivilWar dead, into a day to honor fallen members of the American armed forces from all wars.
(That May 30), 1868 is designated for the purpose of strewing with flowers or otherwise decorating the graves of comrades who died in the defense of their country during the late rebellion, and whose bodies now lie in almost every city, village, and hamlet churchyard in the land.
The national observance of #DecorationDay, in part, is traced to an order by Maj. Gen. John A. Logan, the commander in chief of the Grand Army of the Republic, a fraternal organization of #CivilWar Union veterans. On May 5, 1868, Logan instructed members of the GAR that:
On both sides of the #CivilWar conflict, north and south, families and brothers-in-arms of the fallen came together in grassroots commemorations to lay flowers on the graves of the dead, in honor of their sacrifice. 		This day of remembrance was initially known as #DecorationDay.
#DidYouKnow that #MemorialDay, the federal holiday in which we honor those who died while in the armed services, originated in the aftermath of the #CivilWar?		#HonorThem		(@USArmy painting by Ellen White, courtesy of @USArmyCMH) https://t.co/Lm9G7ZgS26

The memorial was re-dedicated on its 85th anniversary after a thorough restoration.
At the foot of the pyramid is a bronze seagull, made by famed sculptor Gaston Lachaise, to exemplify the zeal and persistence of the @USCG in its duties. Below that is the #CoastGuard motto Semper Paratus, or "Always Ready."
Designed by architect George Howe, each side of the memorial is inscribed with the names of Coast Guardsmen lost at sea during #WWI.
The first occurred 9/17/1918, when 11 shipmates from @USCG Cutter Seneca perished when the torpedoed UK steamer they were assisting sank in the Bay of Biscay. Just 9 days later, Cutter Tampa was sunk by enemy submarine UB-91 in the British Channel, and all on board were lost.
#OnThisDay in 1928, the U.S. Coast Guard Memorial was dedicated. Sitting atop a hill in Section 4 of Arlington National Cemetery, this memorial is dedicated to two tragic episodes in @USCG #WorldWarI history.		#WWI100 #HonorRememberExplore		(@USArmy photo by Elizabeth Fraser) https://t.co/KVSvsIb6Aq

That way, our field operations team will not run them over while using machinery, which could cause damage to equipment or harm someone. 		It helps keep Arlington National Cemetery a safe place to work and visit.
If you are planning on leaving a coin at a headstone here at Arlington (or any cemetery), please place them on top of a headstone instead of throwing them.
This is why you will also see coins left on graves of non-military members, and why many members of the general public will leave coins in any denomination (often what they have on hand) on headstones of loved ones, historical figures, or a headstone that caught their eye.
The tradition of leaving coins is not limited to the military though; it has become a way for anyone to show that they were thinking of someone.
A penny means you know someone who was associated with the deceased (relative). A nickel means they trained with the deceased. A dime says they served with the deceased. A quarter means they were present or assigned with the deceased when they lost their life.
Within the military, there is a tradition of leaving coins on headstones of deceased service members to show respect and remembrance. 		Different coins can mean different things.

During his service, Maj. Fred Ohr earned the #SilverStar twice, the #DistinguishedFlyingCross twice, a #BronzeStar Medal and the #AirMedal 19 times.
After #WWII ended, Maj. Fred Ohr returned to college in California. He studied at UCBerkley and Northwestern Dental School in Chicago. He established a career as a dental surgeon in Chicago.		(Photo courtesy of https://t.co/zFNVhK9Ixv) https://t.co/ZuIAADgVIn
Maj. Fred Ohr served with: 2nd Fighter Squadron, 52nd Fighter Group; 116th Cavalry Regiment, @IDNationalGuard; 183rd Field Artillery; 115th Cavalry; 12th Air Force; 15th Air Force; and 68th Material Service Squadron.
Maj. Fred Ohr scored his first aerial combat victory flying a Spitfire in 1943. The rest of his victories came after the group transitioned to P-51s in 1944. 		He ended his tour of duty as the squadron's commanding officer.
Maj. Fred Ohr was born in Portland, Oregon, in 1919, and became an aviation cadet in 1941. 		During #WorldWarII, he was later assigned to the 2nd Fighter Squadron, 52nd Fighter Group in North Africa and Sicily as a fighter pilot flying Spitfires.
In honor of #AsianPacificAmericanHeritageMonth, today we are highlighting @USArmy Air Forces Maj. Fred F. Ohr, who in 1941 was the first and only American fighter ace of Korean descent.		#AAPI #HonorThem		(Photo courtesy of the American Air Museum in Britain) https://t.co/dzRayaGdvZ
Cmdr. Elmer Stone is laid to rest not far from the #CoastGuardMemorial in Section 4, Grave 3205-A.		#HonorThem
Information for this thread courtesy of https://t.co/HcLq0uEQJA.
For his service, Cmdr. Elmer Stone earned the #NavyCross, the @RoyalAirForce Cross, the Congressional Gold Medal and Portuguese Order of the Tower and Sword.
At one @USCG air station stands a monument to him, with the apt quotation: "A prophet is not without honor." Eccentric perhaps, and something of a trial for his seniors, but he left behind a rich tradition, the foundation for morale for #CoastGuard aviators who follow him.
Cmdr. Elmer Stone is now recognized to have been ahead of his time in many things to the extent that he did not receive the deserved support of more conservative officers.
One officer who knew Cmdr. Elmer Stone well describes him as "pop-eyed, bushy-haired, stub-nosed, careless of dress but as alert as a terrier; a man who cared little for the form, but much for the matter."
Cmdr. Elmer Stone‚Äôs uncanny foresight and ability in aviation, his fine qualities of leadership, made him respected and beloved by all who served under him.
Cmdr. Elmer Stone was the @USCG‚Äôs first aviator and the pilot of the NC-4 on the first successful trans-Atlantic flight in May 1919, eight years before Charles Lindbergh‚Äôs famous solo crossing in the Spirit of Saint Louis.

#ArmedForcesDay is celebrated annually on the third Saturday of May.
On August 31, 1949, #SecDef Louis Johnson announced the creation of an #ArmedForcesDay to replace separate @USAirForce, @USArmy, @USMC and @USNavy days. The single day celebration stemmed from the unification of the armed forces under the @DeptofDefense.
President Harry S. Truman led the effort to establish a single holiday for citizens to come together and thank our military members for their patriotic service in support of our country.
Today is #ArmedForcesDay! 		Pictured are Airmen from then-Andrews Air Force Base, Maryland (now @JBA_NAFW), salute President Harry S. Truman during a parade down Constitution Ave. in @WashingtonDC, for Armed Forces Day in 1951.		#HonorThem		(Photo courtesy of the @DeptOfDefense) https://t.co/imSBQ8W34e
Lt. Col. Dick Scobee is laid to rest in Section 46, Grave 1129-4. 		#HonorThem
Information for this thread courtesy of https://t.co/WaFlxqNQz7
Lt. Col. Dick Scobee eventually logged a total of 168-hours in space.
Lt. Col. Dick Scobee was selected as an astronaut candidate by @NASA in January 1978.  In August 1979, he completed a 1-year training and evaluation period, making him eligible for assignment as a pilot on future space shuttle flightcrews.
After graduating in 1972, Lt. Col. Dick Scobee participated in test programs for which he flew such varied aircraft as the Boeing 747, the X-24B, the transonic aircraft technology (TACT) F-111, and the C-5. He logged more than 6,500 hours flying time in 45 types of aircraft.
Lt. Col. Dick Scobee returned to the United States and attended the @USAirForce Aerospace Research Pilot School at @EdwardsAFB, California.
Lt. Col. Dick Scobee graduated from the @UofA with a bachelor of science degree in Aerospace Engineering.  He received his commission in 1965 and, after receiving his wings in 1966, completed a number of assignments including a combat tour during the #VietnamWar.
While there, Lt. Col. Dick Scobee attended night school and acquired 2 years of college credit which led to his selection for the Airman‚Äôs Education and Commissioning Program.
Lt. Col. Dick Scobee enlisted in the Air Force in 1957, trained as a reciprocating engine mechanic, and was subsequently stationed at Kelly Air Force Base, Texas.
Also killed were Gregory B. Jarvis, S. Christa McAuliffe, Dr. Ronald E. McNair, @USAirForce Lt. Col. Ellison Onizuka, Dr. Judith A. Resnik, and @USNavy Capt. Michael J. Smith.
Lt. Col. Dick Scobee was one of the seven crew members aboard the Space Shuttle #Challenger when it exploded Jan. 28, 1986, just seconds after takeoff.

The history of our nation can be seen every day ‚Äì America's heroes are buried here from every American conflict, from the #RevolutionaryWar to the conflicts of the 21st century.
What was once created out of necessity during the #CivilWar, Arlington National Cemetery is a national shrine for hundreds of thousands active duty military members, veterans and their families who have served during times of war and times of peace.

Fleet Adm. William Leahy is laid to rest in Section 2, Grave 932.		#HonorThem
Biographical information for this thread courtesy of https://t.co/OWjBadyvEO.
Fleet Adm. William Leahy was promoted to Admiral of the Fleet in late 1944.
Fleet Adm. William Leahy became closer to President Franklin D. Roosevelt and accompanied him to the Pacific Strategy Conference in Hawaii and at Yalta in 1945 with Winston Churchill and Joseph Stalin. 		#WorldWarII
In #WorldWarII, President Franklin D. Roosevelt brought Fleet Adm. William Leahy out of retirement as the first-ever (though not in name) chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff and his own personal chief of staff.
As a retiree, Fleet Adm. William Leahy was governor of Puerto Rico and @StateDept ambassador to France.
In the period between the wars, Fleet Adm. William Leahy held a variety of posts within the @USNavy, culminating in his tenure as Chief of Naval Operations, then retirement.
In April of 1918 during #WorldWarI, Fleet Adm. William Leahy got command of the Princess Matoika, which was transporting American troops to France. 		#WWI100
In 1915, Fleet Adm. William Leahy was commanding the Dolphin, a dispatch gunboat, when he became friends with the assistant secretary of the Navy, Franklin D. Roosevelt, who cruised on the ship.
#TodayIRemember @USNavy Fleet Adm. William D. Leahy, who was born #OnThisDay in 1875.		Who are you remembering today?		#HonorThem		(Headstone photo by Rachel Larue, Arlington National Cemetery; portraits courtesy of @USNHistory) https://t.co/8uLhDWlgb8

By the 1960s, when the #CivilRights movement gathered momentum, Bill Mauldin had further honed his skills as a cartoonist. 		(Comic circa 1962, from the estate of William Mauldin, courtesy of the @LibraryCongress) https://t.co/l40YVbfIqq
Bill Mauldin was a champion of the oppressed and soon after his return to the United States in 1945 he began attacking segregationists and the Ku Klux Klan.
In 1958, Bill Mauldin joined the @STLToday, winning his second #PulitzerPrize. 		In 1961, he was awarded the @NatCartoonSoc‚Äôs #ReubenAward as cartoonist of the year.

During #WorldWarII, Bill Mauldin earned fame for the cartoon exploits of his two unshaven, weary, but wry, @USArmy infantrymen as they fought Hitler's Wehrmacht troops across Europe. 		(Comic circa 1943, courtesy of @USArmyCMH) https://t.co/BxMZZXjHFU

On headstones and niche covers, the #SilverStar is typically spelled out but it can also be abbreviated SS, as shown on @USArmy Maj. Audie Murphy's headstone in Section  46.		(#USArmy photo by Rachel Larue, Arlington National Cemetery) https://t.co/PKLGtLfHkY
The #SilverStar is awarded for gallantry in action: or while serving with friendly foreign forces engaged in an armed conflict against an opposing armed force in which the United States is not a belligerent party. (3/3)
The #SilverStar is awarded for gallantry in action: while engaged in military operations involving conflict with an opposing foreign force; (2/3)
The #SilverStar is awarded for gallantry in action while engaged in action against an enemy of the United States; (1/3)
#DidYouKnow today is #SilverStar Banner Day? The Silver Star is the third-highest military combat decoration that can be awarded to a member of the U.S. armed forces.		(Photo of the Silver Star Medal courtesy of @USNHistory) https://t.co/0c1MsmonfE

The honors joined a host of others earned as a naval aviator including the Navy Distinguished Flying Cross, the Navy Commendation Medal with Valor, the Navy Unit Citation, the Vietnam Cross of Gallantry with Silver Star, among others.
Capt. Michael J. Smith was awarded the Space Medal of Honor and the Defense Distinguished Service Medal, as well as promotion to the rank of captain, all posthumously.
The mission was to deploy a communications satellite intended to form part of the Tracking and Data Relay Satellite System. Something went terribly wrong 73 seconds into the Challenger's 10th flight and the space shuttle exploded on live television before a shocked nation.
During his time at @NASA, Capt. Michael J. Smith served in a variety of capacities. Five years later, on a cold January day in Florida, Capt. Michael J. Smith was ready for his first space flight onboard the Space Shuttle #Challenger.
In May of 1980, Capt. Michael J. Smith got his chance to pursue his ultimate dream and was selected as an astronaut candidate by the @NASA. He completed his one year training and evaluation period in August 1981, qualifying him for assignment as a pilot on a space shuttle.
In 1969, as astronaut Neil Armstrong made fame with his ‚Äú‚Ä¶small step for  man, giant leap for mankind‚Äù walk on the moon, Capt. Michael J. Smith received his naval  aviator wings of gold.
Capt. Michael J. Smith went on to graduate with a master‚Äôs degree in aeronautical engineering from the @NPS_Monterey in California.
Rather than considering a career as a commercial pilot, Capt. Michael J. Smith decided he would challenge himself as a naval aviator. He was accepted  to the @NavalAcademy where he graduated in 1967 with degree in Naval  Science.
Capt. Michael J. Smith grew up in Beaufort, North Carolina, during the 1950s and 60s, intrigued by watching the planes landing and taking off from the airstrip adjacent to his family‚Äôs farm. Even then he knew what he wanted, he wanted to fly.
Also killed were Gregory B. Jarvis, S. Christa McAuliffe, Dr. Ronald E.  McNair, @USAirForce Lt. Col. Ellison Onizuka, Dr. Judith A. Resnik, and #AirForce Lt. Col. Francis R. "Dick" Scobee.
Capt. Michael J. Smith was one of the seven crew members aboard the Space Shuttle Challenger when it exploded Jan. 28, 1986, shortly after takeoff.

The #Empress tree is known for its extremely large leaves and pale violet flowers, which typically appear in late spring.
Following its introduction to the U.S., the #Empress tree has become popular because of its highly decorative floral blooms as well as its ability to grow extremely quickly.
According to @Yale University's Nature Walk, the empress tree, also commonly known as the foxglvoe, princess, or kiri tree, originated from western and central China.
Our #Empress tree, or Paulownia tomentosa, is a state champion tree located in Section 46, near Gravesite 305.
The Virginia Big Tree Program is managed by @Virginia_Tech.
If two trees score within five points of each other, they are designated co-champions.
In order to be considered a champion, co-champion, or to make the #Top5 list for a tree species, the tree needs to rank highest with regards to tree height, crown spread, and trunk circumference.

Mary Randolph along with her husband, ran a boardinghouse in Richmond, and it was there that she gained a reputation for her culinary skills. In 1825, she published one of the best-known cookbooks of the nineteenth century, the Virginia House-Wife.
Mary Randolph was the cousin of George Washington Parke Custis' wife, and the first recorded person buried on the grounds that became Arlington National Cemetery. She was also the sister of #ThomasJefferson's daughter Martha's husband Thomas Mann Randolph.

The #SUBSAFE program does this by enforcing a set of stringent quality processes and material control requirements beginning in the design phase and enforced throughout the submarine's entire service life.
This tragic loss led directly to the establishment of the #SUBSAFE program, which is known worldwide as a model for safety and quality assurance. The SUBSAFE program works to ensure that submarines do not experience flooding events; if they do, they have the ability to surface.
#OnThisDay in 1963, the #USSThresher sank off the coast of Massachusetts during a deep-diving test, making it the first nuclear-powered submarine lost at sea and the largest loss of life in the submarine force's history. 		#HonorThem		(Photo courtesy of @USNHistory) https://t.co/oXc5mw3bSV
All five brothers have Memorial Markers next to each other in Section MC, Sites 30-34, on Porter Drive.	   	#HonorThem
Since the tragic loss of the Sullivan brothers, the U.S. military has revised its policy on allowing siblings to serve under the same command.
A common peace-time practice in the @USNavy, which was never specifically forbidden during #WorldWarII, was for brothers to serve together on the same ship.
The five Sullivan brothers who died were @USNavy Seaman 2nd Class Albert Leo Sullivan, Seaman 2nd Class Madison Abel Sullivan, Seaman 2nd Class Joseph Eugene Sullivan, Coxswain Francis Henry Sullivan, and Gunner's Mate 2nd Class George Thomas Sullivan.
All five Sullivan brothers were assigned to the #USSJuneau, which sunk after being hit by a torpedo.		Of the 700 crew members aboard the USS Juneau, approximately 100 survived the initial torpedo attack and only 10 were rescued from the water eight days after the sinking.

1st Lt. James Reese Europe is laid to rest in Section 1, Grave 3576.	   	#HonorThem	   	(Photo courtesy of Arlington National Cemetery) https://t.co/LuMkMKIahC
Information for this thread courtesy of Rudi Williams, American Forces Press Service. Full article available at https://t.co/8QXjirzJvL.
R. Reid Badger noted in his book "A Life in Ragtime" that 1st Lt. James Reese Europe received the first public funeral for a black man in New York City May 13, 1919. Thousands of fans, black and white, turned out to pay their respect.
A deranged drummer named Herbert Wright cut Europe's jugular vein with a penknife while the bandleader was preparing for a show at Mechanics Hall in Boston. Wright had been angry because he thought Europe favored his twin brother over him.
1st Lt. James Reese Europe ironically survived being shot at and gassed in the trenches of France only to die on May 9, 1919, at the hands of one of his own men.
Back in America, 1st Lt. James Reese Europe found himself even more popular than before he went to war. He recorded "One Patrol in No Man's Land"; it became a nationwide hit.
When the regiment returned home in the spring of 1919, it paraded up New York's 5th Avenue to Harlem led by the band, playing its raggedy tunes to the delight of more than a million spectators. 	   	(Photo courtesy of the @USNatArchives) https://t.co/udeovZ7xT4
The band also performed for high-ranking military and civilian officials and for French citizens in cities across France. After Germany surrendered, the #HarlemHellfighters Band became popular performing throughout Europe.
1st Lt. James Reese Europe and his musicians were ordered in August 1918 to entertain thousands of Soldiers in camps and hospitals. Here they are pictured performing for wounded U.S. service members in Paris hospital courtyard.	   	(Photo courtesy of the @LibraryCongress) https://t.co/URl3pt2fCM
Trained to command a machine gun company, 1st Lt. James Reese Europe  learned to fire French machine guns and became the first American  officer and first African-American to lead troops in battle during the  war.
When the 369th and its band arrived in France, they were assigned to the 16th "Le Gallais" Division of the @armee2terre, or French Army's Fourth Army because white U.S. Army units refused to fight alongside them.
1st Lt. James Reese Europe also recruited singers, comedians, dancers and others who could entertain troops. 	   	He recruited the best drum major he could find -- Harlem dancer Bill "Bojangles" Robinson (pictured).	   	(Photo courtesy of the @LibraryCongress) https://t.co/JHImoDX0rS
1st Lt. James Reese Europe even recruited woodwind players from Puerto Rico because there weren't enough in Harlem.
With the promise of extra money to attract first-class musicians, 1st Lt. James Reese Europe recruited musicians from Harlem and reportedly put together one of the finest military bands that ever existed.
1st Lt. James Reese Europe's regimental commander, Col. William Hayward, asked the new lieutenant to organize "the best damn brass band in the United States Army."
1st Lt. James Reese Europe enlisted as a private in @USArmy Sept. 18, 1916. 		Europe accomplished something only a few African-Americans did in those days: He attended officers training and was commissioned a lieutenant.
On May 2, 1912, 1st Lt. James Reese Europe's Clef Club Orchestra became the first African-American band and the first jazz band to play in New York City's famous @CarnegieHall.
1st Lt. James Reese Europe's popularity soared as a bandleader and arranger for the internationally acclaimed dance duo Irene and Vernon Castle. The Castles and Europe helped pioneer modern dance by popularizing the foxtrot and other dances.
1st Lt. James Reese Europe helped found an African-American fraternity known as "the Frogs," and, in 1910, established the Clef Club, the first African-American music union and booking agency.
1st Lt. James Reese Europe moved to New York City in 1903 to pursue a musical career. Work as a violinist was scarce, so he turned to the piano and found work in several cabarets.
According to books about ragtime and early jazz, 1st Lt. James Reese Europe was the most respected black bandleader of the "teens" when the United States entered #WorldWarI.
The French government called 1st Lt. James Reese Europe a battlefield hero. Before the war, however, he was a household name in New York's music world and on the dance scene nationwide.
1st Lt. James Reese Europe was an unrelenting fighter for the dignity of African-American musicians and for them to be paid on the same scale as their white peers.
1st Lt. James Reese Europe is credited with bringing ragtime out of the bordellos and juke joints into mainstream society and elevating African-American music into an accepted art form.
In honor of #JazzAppreciationMonth, today we are highlighting @USArmy 1st Lt. James Reese Europe, bandmaster of the @NationalGuardNY's 369th Infantry Regiment, also known as the #HarlemHellfighers. 	   	(Photo courtesy of the @LibraryCongress) https://t.co/JIzYmQmgJx

Gen. Omar Bradley is laid to rest in Section 30, Grave 428-1-2.	   	#HonorThem
Information for this thread courtesy of https://t.co/hZTsTNzzL9.
After Gen. Omar Bradley left active duty, both political and military leaders continued to seek Bradley's advice. Perhaps more importantly, he remained in close touch with the Army and served its succeeding generations as the ideal model of a professional Soldier.
That same concern made Gen. Omar Bradley the ideal choice in 1945 to reinvigorate the  Veterans Administration and prepare it to meet the needs of millions of demobilized servicemen.
Self-effacing and quiet, Gen. Omar Bradley showed a concern for the men he led that gave him the reputation as the "Soldier's general."
Gen. Omar Bradley had also more than made up for his lack of combat duty, for during #WorldWarII he successively commanded a division, a corps, an army, and finally a group of armies.
Thirty-five years later Gen. Omar Bradley held the highest rank in the @USArmy, had been its Chief of Staff, and had served two terms as the first Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff.
At the end of #WorldWarI, Gen. Omar Bradley considered himself a professional failure because he had spent the war in the United States while his contemporaries had distinguished themselves on the battlefields of France. His gloomy self-assessment was premature.
#TodayIRemember @USArmy Gen. Omar Bradley, who died #OnThisDay in 1981. Bradley served as the General of the Army and first Chairman of @TheJointStaff.		Who are you remembering today?		#HonorThem		(Headstone photo by Rachel Larue; portraits courtesy of @USArmyCMH). https://t.co/lMcppAgwi8

Information for this thread is courtesy of the @MedicalMuseum at https://t.co/FYs3rfvxog.
In 1931, Congress awarded the yellow fever volunteers and doctors the ‚ÄúConquest of Yellow Fever‚Äù medal, as well as pension of $125 a month. Several of the awards, including Maj. Walter Reed‚Äôs, were awarded posthumously.
During his hospital stay, Pvt. Charles Gustave Sonntag lost 50 pounds and felt the effects of the disease for the rest of his life. Sonntag eventually returned to South Carolina and settled in Gaston, where he married and become a farmer.
After being exposed and bitten by an infected mosquito, Pvt. Charles Gustave Sonntag became ill with yellow fever and was admitted to the hospital on Feb. 10, 1901, and was discharged on March 12, 1901.
Maj. Walter Reed's work, based upon the theories of Cuban doctor Carlos Finlay, eventually proved the hypothesis that mosquitoes transmitted the devastating disease.
Throughout the late nineteenth century, #YellowFever epidemics routinely swept through the southern United States and during the #SpanishAmericanWar, losses from yellow fever were thirteen times higher than deaths from military operations.
Pvt. Charles Gustave Sonntag was a member of the South Carolina Infantry Regiment and served during the #SpanishAmericanWar. He along with the other volunteers knew exposure to yellow fever could result in death, but their sacrifice was made ‚Äúfor the sake of humanity.‚Äù

According to the @MortonArboretum, yellowwood trees tend to be a medium- to large-sized tree, 30-50 feet high, with smooth bark, large hanging clusters of fragrant white flowers, and clear yellow fall color. They are native to North Carolina, Kentucky and Tennessee.
Our yellowwood, or Cladrastis kentukea, is a champion tree and grows in Section 23, near the Memorial Amphitheater.
The Virginia Big Tree Program is managed by @Virginia_Tech.
If two trees score within five points of each other, they are designated co-champions.
In order to be considered a champion, co-champion, or to make the #Top5 list for a tree species, the tree needs to rank highest with regards to tree height, crown spread, and trunk circumference.

Sentinels wear the @USArmy dress blue uniform, reminiscent of the color and style worn by Soldiers of the late 1800s.
Why 21? 		The 21 is symbolic of the highest salute accorded to dignitaries in military and state ceremonies. As a gesture against intrusion, the sentinel always bears his weapon away from the Tomb.
While on duty, the sentinel crosses a 63-foot rubber surfaced walkway in 21 steps. He faces the Tomb for 21 seconds, turns again and pauses an additional 21 seconds before retracing his steps.
#OnThisDay in 1948, the @USArmyOldGuard assumed the post for the security of the #TombOfTheUnknownSoldier, following the unit‚Äôs reactivation in @WashingtonDC. 		Only exemplary Soldiers serve as sentinels at the Tomb. 		(@USArmy file photo by Martha C. Koester, @NCOJournal) https://t.co/PxhQo7p4N9

The surviving Family members wear either a Gold Star Lapel Button or the Lapel Button for Next of Kin of Deceased Personnel in honor of their fallen Soldier. Family members typically receive these pins from the Army before or after their Soldier's funeral service.
In 1973, the Lapel Button for Next of Kin of Deceased Personnel was  introduced to symbolize active duty Soldiers who lost their lives in non-combat operations.
In 1947, #Congress approved the design, manufacture and distribution of the official Gold Star Lapel Button, a symbol worn by next of kin of service members of the armed forces who lost their lives in war and armed hostilities of the United States.
Per guidance by the @USArmy, the day is now observed as #GoldStarSpousesDay.		#DidYouKnow the Gold Star is a symbol of loss dating back to #WorldWarI?

Vice Adm. Joel T. Boone is laid to rest in Section 11, Grave 137-2.		#HonorThem
Vice Adm. Joel T. Boone was one of three officers selected to liberate American prisoners of war in Japan even before the surrender documents were signed, and represented @NavyMedicine during those ceremonies on the deck of the #USSMissouri.
Vice Adm. Joel T. Boone first earned his keep as the assistant @WhiteHouse physician to Presidents Warren G. Harding and Calvin Coolidge, and the primary care physician to President Herbert Hoover.
In the 1920s and 30s, Vice Adm. Joel T. Boone was seen by many as the consummate, and perceivably perennial, practitioner of @WhiteHouse medicine.
Vice Adm. Joel T. Boone is a #MedalOfHonor recipient noted for heroics in France and Haiti; he is credited for adapting helodecks to @USNavy hospital ships, reforming health and sanitary conditions in coal-mines and the namesake of a @NMCP1 clinic in Virginia.
Vice Adm. Joel T. Boone is a legendary figure in Navy medical lore.

#DidYouKnow pin oak acorns are eaten by many songbirds, wild turkeys, white-tailed deer, squirrels and smaller rodents, but are a particularly important food for many ducks?
Because of its tolerance for wet conditions, the tree is also known regionally as swamp oak, water oak and swamp Spanish oak.
Our pin oak, or Quercus palustris, is a co-champion tree and is located in Section 35, near Grave 1339. According to the @ArborDay Foundation, the name pin oak comes from its short, tough branchlets located along the branches and limbs.
The Virginia Big Tree Program is managed by @Virginia_Tech.
If two trees score within five points of each other, they are designated co-champions.
In order to be considered a champion, co-champion, or to make the #Top5 list for a tree species, the tree needs to rank highest with regards to tree height, crown spread, and trunk circumference.
In honor of #ArborDay coming up (observed nationally the last Friday in April), today we're highlighting one of the cemetery's five Virginia state champion and co-champion trees: the pin oak.	   	(@USArmy photo by Rachel Larue) https://t.co/MSba3gsoJ8

Capt. Joy Bright Hancock is laid to rest in Section 30, Grave 2138-RH.		#HonorThem		(@USArmy photo courtesy of Arlington National Cemetery) https://t.co/kN2DDwwOfC
Information for this thread courtesy of @USNHistory and Heritage Command -- full source available at https://t.co/4IDkUd1if5.
Capt. Joy Bright Hancock retired from active duty in June 1953, and passed away Aug. 20, 1986.
Following #WWII, Capt. Joy Bright Hancock served as Director of the Women‚Äôs Reserve and then Assistant Chief of Personnel for Women.  She was instrumental in preparing the Women‚Äôs Armed Services Integration Act for passage.
Capt. Joy Bright Hancock received commendations for her service to the Bureau of Naval Aeronautics and the Chief of Naval Operations (Air) during #WorldWarII and for her assistance in expanding opportunities for females in the @USNavy.
Capt. Joy Bright Hancock was one of eight women to be sworn into the regular @USNavy following the Women‚Äôs Armed Service Integration Act of 1948.
In the first year of #WorldWarII, Capt. Joy Bright Hancock was commissioned as a lieutenant in the @USNavyReserve's Women‚Äôs Reserve eventually advancing to the rank of captain in July 1946.
Following #WorldWarI, Capt. Joy Bright Hancock worked as a civilian employee for the @USNavy.¬†Between 1934 and 1942 she was the civilian head of Editorial and Research Section of the Navy Bureau of Aeronautics and the Special Assistant to the Bureau‚Äôs Chief.
During #WorldWarI, Capt. Joy Bright Hancock was a Yeoman First Class on duty at the New York Shipbuilding Corporation in Camden, New Jersey.¬† By then end of the war she was Chief Yeoman at the U.S. Naval Air Station in Cape May, New Jersey.
Capt. Joy Bright Hancock earned degrees from @GWtweets and the Crawford School of Foreign Service in Washington, D.C.; the Pierce School of Business Administration in Philadelphia; and the Paris Branch of the New York School of Fine Arts.
Capt. Joy Bright Hancock was among the first wave of females allowed to serve in the @USNavy and was instrumental in advancing their continued integration into that service.
In honor of #WomensHistoryMonth, today we are highlighting @USNavy Capt. Joy Bright Hancock.		(Photo courtesy of @USNHistory and Heritage Command) https://t.co/XUxSkjjg7j

Maj. Walter Reed is laid to rest in Section 3, Grave 1864.	   	#HonorThem	   	(@USArmy photo by Elizabeth Fraser, Arlington National Cemetery) https://t.co/D41N5uQaYV
Information for this thread courtesy of https://t.co/erkyEIKhNJ.
Although Maj. Walter Reed died more than a century ago, his name and legacy persist. 		#DidYouKnow that the @WRBethesda Medical Center in Bethesda, Maryland, is named for him?
As the consent form shows, researchers wanted to be certain that all  volunteers understood the potential hazards of the research.
The 1900 #YellowFever Commission, headed by Maj. Walter Reed, was the first recorded use of informed consent in human research. 	   	(Document courtesy of the @MedicalMuseum) https://t.co/G8tItFkA59
In 1898, Maj. Walter Reed investigated the typhoid fever epidemic, expanding our understanding of how this disease spreads. Just a year later, he described how yellow fever, one of the most dreaded tropical diseases, is transmitted.
In 1893, Maj. Walter Reed became curator of the Army Medical Museum (now the @MedicalMuseum) and professor of clinical microscopy at the Army Medical School (now the @WRAIR).
Maj. Walter Reed's research into the causes of typhoid and yellow fever has saved countless human lives.
In honor of #DoctorsDay, today we're highlighting @USArmy Maj. Walter Reed, who was a pioneering bacteriologist and led experiments establishing mosquito transmission of #YellowFever.		(@USArmy photo) https://t.co/YnOybijAmh

Our apologies -- the #VietnamVeterans #MemorialTree is in Section 28, not 33.
The Kennedy grave site is now paved with irregular stones of #CapeCod granite with sedum planted in between, to resemble stone lying naturally in a Massachusetts field.
During the first three years after President John F. Kennedy‚Äôs death, more than 16 million people visited his gravesite. Because of the large crowds, Kennedy‚Äôs family and cemetery officials decided a more suitable site should be constructed, which is what is seen today.
The initial plot was 20 x 30 feet and surrounded by a white picket fence. First Lady Jacqueline B. Kennedy lit the eternal flame during the funeral. The flame burns from the center of a 5-foot circular flat-granite stone at the head of the grave, seen in the black &amp; white image.
The location was selected partly based on President John F. Kennedy‚Äôs previous visit to the cemetery March 3, 1963. After touring the @ArlingtonNPS the president remarked that the view of @WashingtonDC was so magnificent that he could stay forever.
President John F. Kennedy was laid to rest with a state funeral and full military honors at #ArlingtonNationalCemetery on Nov. 25, 1963, on the slope below #ArlingtonHouse.
On Nov. 22, 1963, when he was hardly past his first thousand days in office, President John F. Kennedy was assassinated as his motorcade wound through Dallas.

Maj. Gen. Adolphus Greely is laid to rest in Section 1, Grave 129.	   	#HonorThem
#DidYouKnow #FortGreely, the #USArmy base in Alaska is named for Maj. Gen. Adolphus Greely? Greely founded the Alaska Communications System, and was responsible for the construction of thousands of miles of telegraph lines throughout the U.S., Puerto Rico, and the Philippines.
From 1887-1906, Maj. Gen. Adolphus Greely was the @USArmy's Chief Signal Officer and brought the #SignalCorps into the age of modern technology. 		During his tenure he introduced the radio, automobile, and the airplane into military use.
Maj. Gen. Adolphus Greely was an explorer who survived two years of arctic winters without being resupplied in 1881. He also founded the @NationalGeographic Society, and was awarded the #MedalOfHonor when he was 91 years old.

Vinnie Ream Hoxie was the first woman artist to be commissioned by the government, and the last artist whom Lincoln sat for before his death. 		In 1878, she married @USArmy Lt. Richard Hoxie. She also sculpted many other statues including Sappho, the poetess, above her grave.

Jane Delano is laid to rest in Section 21, Grave 6.		#HonorThem		(@USArmy photo by Elizabeth Fraser, Arlington National Cemetery) https://t.co/hKx6sIT7u8
Jane Delano was posthumously awarded the #DistinguishedServiceMedal for her work in engineering a supply of 21,000 professional nurse to serve during #WorldWarI.
Jane Delano served as the second superintendent from Aug. 12, 1909-March 31, 1912. She devoted herself to reorganizing and improving the @ArmyNurseCorps and to increasing the pay to attract the best class of nurses.
When Jane Delano was offered the superintendency of the @ArmyNurseCorps, she accepted, believing that it would bring a closer relationship between the nursing services of the Army and the @RedCross.
During the #SpanishAmericanWar Jane Delano joined the @RedCross and became interested in securing nurses for enrollment in the Red Cross Nursing Section.
Jane Delano graduated from @NYCHealthSystem‚Äôs Bellevue Hospital in 1886 and returned as superintendent in 1897. In that decade, besides other posts, she served as superintendent of a yellow fever hospital.
In honor of #WomensHistoryMonth, today we are highlighting Jane Delano, a @USArmy nurse who greatly contributed to the nursing profession. 		(Photo courtesy of @AMEDDMuseum) https://t.co/k9JXm6xeaZ

1st Lt. Vernon Baker died at his St. Maries, Idaho, home on July 13, 2010, after a long battle with cancer.		He is laid to rest in Section 59, Grave 4408.		#HonorThem		(@USArmy file photo courtesy of Arlington National Cemetery) https://t.co/6slpZOsF1W
Information for this thread courtesy of https://t.co/DwwWkExVke.
After his induction into the Pentagon‚Äôs Hall of Heroes in 1997, 1st Lt. Vernon Baker chats with former Army Chief of Staff Gen. Dennis J. Reimer and former Sgt. Maj. of the Army Gene C. McKinney. 		(Photo courtesy of the @DeptofDefense) https://t.co/ZXzqZmhBf0
Six other African-American service members were recognized with the award that year but four had died in action and the other two had died in the decades after the war.
On Jan. 13, 1997, President Bill Clinton presented 1st Lt. Vernon Baker with the #MedalOfHonor, he was the only living African-American #WorldWarII veteran to earn it.
In 1996, more than 50 years after his actions in Italy, 1st Lt. Vernon Baker received a telephone call explaining that the military was seeking to re-evaluate the heroism of African-Americans in #WorldWarII and he was to receive the #MedalOfHonor.
1st Lt. Vernon Baker remained in the @USArmy until 1968 and became one of the first African-American officers to command an all-white company.
Following the battle, 1st Lt. Vernon Baker was nominated for the nation‚Äôs second-highest honor for battlefield valor, the #DistinguishedServiceCross. At the time, the #MedalOfHonor was reserved for white troops.
1st Lt. Vernon Baker led two attacks against the stronghold, taking out many of the enemy positions in the mountainous terrain. This action secured the position for Baker‚Äôs team and helped to drive enemy forces out of northern Italy.
1st Lt. Vernon Baker and his troops were ordered to launch an early morning assault against a mountain stronghold occupied by the Germans.
In the spring of 1945, 1st Lt. Vernon Baker ‚Äî the only black officer in his company ‚Äî was in command of a weapons unit near Viareggio, Italy. It was just a few months before #WorldWarII would end, but on April 5, the battles were still raging.
1st Lt. Vernon Baker joined the segregated 270th Regiment of the 92nd Infantry Division. He and his fellow soldiers were members of the first African-American unit to go into combat in #WorldWarII.
In honor of #MedalOfHonorDay, we‚Äôd like to highlight @USArmy 1st Lt. Vernon Baker. 		In the summer of 1941, Baker enlisted in the Army. After completing officer candidate school, he was commissioned Jan. 11, 1943.		(#USArmy file photo) https://t.co/KMNLxEqLWL
The #MedalOfHonor is presented to service members who distinguish themselves through conspicuous gallantry and intrepidity at the risk of life above and beyond the call of duty.
#DidYouKnow today is National #MedalOfHonorDay?		The #MedalOfHonor is the highest military decoration that may be awarded by the United States government. https://t.co/9rOF8mmroG

Lt. Col. Juanita Redmond Hipps was one of the few nurses to escape and wrote "I Served on Bataan," a best-seller in 1943, which later became the basis for the movie "So Proudly We Hail," a story about military nurses sent to the Philippines during #WorldWarII.
When Bataan and Corregidor fell, almost 80 nurses were captured and imprisoned.
Pictured in original tweet is then Maj. Juanita Redmond (left), giving Lt. Rosemary Hogan new bars.
