On May 22, 1943, Nellie Taft became the first First Lady to be buried at #ArlingtonNationalCemetery.		She is laid to rest with her husband, President William Howard Taft, in Section 30, Grave S-14.		(@USArmy photo by Elizabeth Fraser) https://t.co/c5VQuWwRPO
Information from this thread courtesy of https://t.co/EEjckhHWVm and https://t.co/D4XYnqiDMg.
In May 1912 Nellie Taft donated her inaugural gown to the @Smithsonian, the founding jewel in its popular #FirstLadies exhibit.
#DidYouKnow that in 1912 Nellie Taft became the first First Lady to initiate a federal act, Executive Order 1498, for Bureau of Public Health inspections of executive branch buildings to secure sanitary and safe conditions for federal employees?
Nellie Taft eventually arranged for the planting of the 3,000 Japanese cherry trees (a gift from Tokyo) that grace the Washington #TidalBasin. The first two saplings were planted March 27, 1912. 		(Postcard printed between 1920 and 1960. Courtesy of the @LibraryCongress) https://t.co/thgStrv5E9
Nellie Taft shared an interest in Japanese culture with Eliza Scidmore, the only female trustee of @NatGeo. In 1909, Scidmore pleaded for the beautification of #PotomacPark with Japanese flowering cherry trees, a request she had been making for the past 24 years.
Within a month Nellie Taft had driven to the new Potomac Park "Speedway," which she promptly renamed Potomac Drive, returning in hours with a Paseo de Luneta-type plan for a #PotomacPark bandstand and a series of concerts.		(@LibraryCongress photo of the Boulevard, Potomac Park) https://t.co/G4QcTLm0gW
Nellie Taft arranged @WhiteHouse concerts, including at a new series of garden  parties, featuring established and hot new virtuosi, performing  classical, folk, popular and lyrical music. She added dramatic performances, including Shakespeare.
Nellie Taft took command of @WhiteHouse domestic and social matters, establishing equal treatment of staff and entertaining a wider scope of visitors, to which she provided entertainment worthy  of a nation.
As First Lady from 1909-1913, Nellie Taft was the most-traveled First Lady to date. She was first to ride with her husband in the inaugural parade, establishing the precedent. 		(@LibraryCongress photo, March 4, 1909) https://t.co/qIlnDJFO7H
Nellie Taft was a lady of "firsts," and was among the First Ladies to transform the role into the substantive one it is today. 		Her legacy is still evident in parts of the @NationalMallNPS; was instrumental in bringing Japanese cherry trees to Washington, D.C.		#CherryBlossomDC

At @GeorgeMasonU, Brig. Gen. Hazel Johnson-Brown was instrumental in founding the Center for Health Policy, designed to educate and involve nurses in health policy and policy design. She retired from teaching in 1997.

RT @LTGWest: The "Letterman Plan" (1863) became law in 1865 and established the  foundation for modern Army medicine including the accounta‚Ä¶

Maj. Jonathan Letterman is laid to rest in Section 3, Grave 1869.		#HonorThem
Information for this thread from https://t.co/mGnZ1TSpTK.
In recognition of the importance of casualty triage, Maj. Jonathan Letterman established three echelons of treatment facilities that we still use today.
Ira Rutkow, a medial historian said Maj. Jonathan Letterman's surgical credentialing on the basis of expertise was ‚Äò‚Äòone of the most momentous medical reforms to come out of the Civil War.‚Äô‚Äô
Maj. Jonathan Letterman implemented a policy of professional privileging based on specialized skills and documented experience; each division's three physicians with the most extensive surgical experience were permitted to perform surgical operations, which was unprecedented.
Maj. Jonathan Letterman made the medical logistics system more efficient by reducing the number of medicines and materials to be carried, thereby decreasing the number of wagons used to transport them by half.
Drivers of the ambulances were required to complete training designed by Maj. Jonathan Letterman to increase their level of commitment + effectiveness; the ambulance system met a previously unfilled need but was of limited capacity + often challenged by the number of casualties.
Maj. Jonathan Letterman developed a system to transport the injured (ultimately used by all Union Armies) in which each regiment was supported by three ambulances, each with two privates and a driver, and one transport cart with a driver.
Maj. Jonathan Letterman immediately placed emphasis on efficient medical supply logistics, the enforcement of sanitary measures, and the prompt removal of the sick and wounded from the battlefield.
Maj. Jonathan Letterman was promoted to full surgeon and given the rank of major in June 1862 before being designated medical director of the Army of the Potomac by Surgeon General William A. Hammond.
In November 1861, Maj. Jonathan Letterman was assigned to the Army of the Potomac, and then in May 1862, was designated medical director of the Department of West Virginia.
Before the #CivilWar, Maj. Jonathan Letterman provided surgical care for casualties in a number of military campaigns against Indian tribes in Florida, New Mexico, and California.
Maj. Jonathan Letterman's efforts to rationalize and organize combat casualty care during the #CivilWar earned him the title of ‚Äò‚ÄòFather of Battlefield Medicine."
#TodayIRemember @USArmy Maj. Jonathan Letterman, who died #OnThisDay in 1872. 		Who are you remembering today?		#HonorThem		(Headstone photo by Elizabeth Fraser, #ArlingtonNationalCemetery; portrait courtesy of @NLM_news) https://t.co/aX1x5EvEX2

Jacqueline Bouvier Kennedy Onassis is laid to rest with her first husband President John F. Kennedy at the Kennedy gravesite in Section 45.	   	(@USArmy photo by Elizabeth Fraser, Arlington National Cemetery) https://t.co/Fvri65NTWv
Information for this thread courtesy of the @JFKLibrary at https://t.co/f6g8BtmKn8.
With a deep sense of devotion to her family and country, Jacqueline Bouvier Kennedy Onassis dedicated herself to raising her children and to making the world a better place through art, literature, and a respect for history and public service.
While she was first lady, she helped to stop the destruction of historic buildings along the square, including the Renwick Building, now part of the @Smithsonian Institution. In New York City, she led a campaign to save and renovate #GrandCentralStation.
Throughout her life, Jacqueline Bouvier Kennedy Onassis sought to preserve and protect America‚Äôs cultural heritage. The results of her work are still visible in Lafayette Square, across from the @WhiteHouse in Washington, DC.
In 1968, Jacqueline Bouvier Kennedy Onassis married Greek shipping magnate Aristotle Onassis. When Mr. Onassis died in 1975, she became a widow a second time. She enjoyed a successful career in publishing at @VikingBooks and @DoubledayBooks until she passed away, May 19, 1994.
Soon after President John F. Kennedy‚Äôs death, Jacqueline Bouvier Kennedy Onassis began the work of creating the @JFKLibrary and Museum as a memorial to her husband. She chose the architect I.M. Pei to design the building, which now stands as a landmark overlooking #BostonHarbor.
Jacqueline Bouvier Kennedy Onassis established a @WhiteHouse Fine Arts Committee, and created the post of White House curator. Gathering outstanding examples of American art and furniture from around the U.S., she restored all the public rooms in the White House.
While serving as first lady, Jacqueline Bouvier Kennedy Onassis wanted people to have a greater appreciation of the history of America's most famous residence and its past inhabitants. Her first major project was to restore and preserve the @WhiteHouse.

At the time of her retirement, at 79 years of age, Rear Adm. Grace Murray Hopper was the oldest commissioned officer in the @USNavy.

Mary Roberts Rinehart is laid to rest in Section 3, Grave 4269.		#HonorThem
Information for this thread taken from the Herbert Hoover Library and Museum Blog. Read full entry and learn more about Mary Roberts Rinehart at https://t.co/LkKNXKc1B7.
When war broke out in Europe in August 1914, Mary Roberts Rinehart served as a war correspondent, covering the conflict. This war correspondent work first introduced her to President Herbert Hoover, and his wife Lou, forming a life-long friendship.
Mary Roberts Rinehart, a nurse by profession, took up writing to supplement her family‚Äôs income. The mystery novels were the most lucrative source of her writing endeavors but she also served as a regular contributor to popular magazines such as the @SatEvePost and @LHJmagazine.
In Mary Roberts Rinehart's 1930 mystery novel, The Door, the butler is the killer, establishing the genre cliche, ‚Äúthe butler did it.‚Äù
#DidYouKnow Mary Roberts Rinehart's 1920 play The Bat inspired the 1930 movie,The Bat Whispers, which became a source of inspiration for comic book artist Bob Kane in the creation of #Batman?
Long before Agatha Christie, P.D. James, and Patricia Cornwell, Mary Roberts Rinehart was America‚Äôs premier female writer of the ‚Äúwho done it.‚Äù She rose to national fame in 1907 with her novel The Circular Staircase.
In honor of #WomensHistoryMonth, today we are highlighting Mary Roberts Rinehart, who was referred to as "America's Mistress of Mystery.‚Äù 		@LibraryCongress photo shows Rinehart out for a stroll with her favorite pet.		#HonorThem https://t.co/oea9a4ufcc

Matthew Henson is laid to rest in Section 8, Grave S-15-1.		#HonorThem
Want to learn more about Matthew Henson? Check out https://t.co/T9Ks1WwOVr or https://t.co/tVbb7gUCLH.
Members of Matthew Henson‚Äôs family attended the ceremony along with many of the explorer's admirers from around the world. The re-interment represented the ultimate national recognition that Henson had so long deserved.
On April 6, 1988, the remains of Matthew Henson and his wife, Lucy Ross Henson, were transported to Washington, D.C., where they were re-interred among other American heroes, and near the grave site of Robert Peary and his wife Josephine Deibitsch Peary.
In 1987, at the request of Dr. S. Allen Counter of @Harvard University, President Ronald Reagan granted permission for Matthew Henson and his wife to be re-interred at #ArlingtonNationalCemetery.
After his death in 1955, Matthew Henson was buried in New York City's Woodlawn Cemetery. In 1968, the body of his wife Lucy Ross Henson was buried nearby.
Matthew Henson was three times refused a pension by Congress, was excluded from the Explorer's Club in which Robert Peary was president, and lastly was not considered for burial at #ArlingtonNationalCemetery at the time of his death.
During his lifetime, Robert Peary received many honors for his achievement. Matthew Henson, however, received comparatively little recognition for his part in the discovery, even though Peary repeatedly acknowledged Henson's indispensability to the expedition's success.
On that historic day, it was Matthew Henson, an African-American, who first reached the Pole and planted the American flag.
On April 6, 1909, two Americans and four Eskimos became the first human beings to set foot on the #NorthPole. The achievement crowned numerous attempts to reach the Pole, over a period of 18 years, by Robert Edwin Peary and Matthew Henson.
#TodayIRemember Matthew Alexander Henson, co-discoverer of the #NorthPole, who passed away #OnThisDay in 1955. Dr. Albert Bruce Sabin is laid to rest in Section 3, Grave 1885-RH. #HonorThem
Dr. Albert Bruce Sabin also received the Presidential Medal of Freedom from Ronald Reagan in 1986, which is highest award that an American civilian can receive.
Dr. Albert Bruce Sabin received the National Medal of Science in 1970 for his work up to and including the development of the oral polio vaccine.
Dr. Albert Bruce Sabin began testing a new polio vaccine on humans, starting with his own family.
During the same time period, Dr. Albert Bruce Sabin discovered that the polio virus attacked and multiplied in the intestines, as opposed to the respiratory tract as was previously believed.
The first inactive polio vaccine, which required an injection administered by a doctor, was created in 1954 by Dr. Jonas Salk.
In the 1930s, the outbreak of polio, which led to paralysis or death, had reached epidemic proportions both in the United States and around the globe.

Existing for more than 30 years, Freedman‚Äôs Village provided housing, education, training for employment, medical care and food for the former slaves.
Historic rendering of Freedman's Village in Arlington, Virginia. 		(Image courtesy of the @ArlingtonVALib) https://t.co/PEtm3EfWeN
The village was run by the Freedmen's Bureau during most of its existence, and at one point employed U.S. Colored Troops to protect fugitive slaves from their former slave owners.
Contrabands was the U.S. government designation for slaves who were freed as Union forces moved South, or who had escaped from local Virginia and Maryland slave owners.
Freedman's Village was established on the southeast portion of the Arlington Estate in June 1863, as a camp for #CivilWar "contrabands."
The tree, which not far from the Ord &amp; Weitzel Gate, is dedicated to the U.S. Colored Troops (the U.S. government designation for African-Americans who served in segregated @USArmy regiments during the #CivilWar), citizens, freed slaves and contrabands of Freedman's Village.
In Section 27 there is a Red Maple tree dedicated to U.S. Colored Troops and Freed Slaves. 		#BlackHistoryMonth #HonorRememberExplore		(@USArmy photos by Elizabeth Fraser, Arlington National Cemetery) https://t.co/KyButS9OPp

Maj. Charles Bassett is laid to rest in Section 4, Grave 195. 	   	Cmdr. Elliot See is laid to rest in Section 4, Grave 208. 	   	#HonorThem
Cmdr. Elliot See, a 38-year-old Texan, had been a flight engineer and test pilot for @GeneralElectric. He joined @NASA in September 1962, part of NASA's second astronaut class. He worked with the teams developing guidance and navigations systems.
Maj. Charles Bassett, 34, joined the third astronaut class after being a @USAirForce pilot. On the ground, he worked with the teams conducting astronaut training and simulations; Gemini IX would be his first assignment.
The Gemini program was teaching @NASA what it needed to know to be able to send crews to the moon. Astronauts were learning to walk and work in zero gravity. Pilots were practicing rendezvous maneuvers and docking spacecraft.
Maj. Charles Bassett and Cmdr. Elliot See were astronauts with @NASA's #Gemini IX program. They were killed in a plane crash at Lambert Field, St. Louis, Missouri.
#TodayIRemember @USAirForce Maj. Charles Arthur Bassett II and @Navy_Reserve Cmdr. Elliot McKay See Jr., who died #OnThisDay in 1966. Camp Montford Point became obsolete after then @USNavy Secretary Francis Matthews ordered the end of racial division in the #USNavy and @USMC in June 1949.
Among the units organized at Camp Montford Point, North Carolina, were the 51st and 52nd Defense Battalions, which were dispatched to the Pacific but saw no combat action, and 11 ammunition and 51 depot companies that did see action.
All African-American units that served in #WorldWarII trained at Camp Montford Point, North Carolina. Pictured are #Marines jumping over an obstacle during basic training. (Image courtesy of the @USNatArchives) https://t.co/StkeOE4c42
Nearly 20,000 African-Americans joined the @USMC in 1942. They didn't receive recruit training at San Diego or Parris Island, but at Camp Montford Point, North Carolina, a segregated training site for African-American Marine recruits.

#ArlingtonNationalCemetery is home to an extremely diverse and significant collection of trees and landscapes. The more than 8,600 trees are comprised of 300 varieties and species. Learn more about them at https://t.co/mgZybtKJWy.
.@virginia_tech manages the Virginia Big Tree Program and @ArlingtonVA manages a more local Arlington County Champion Tree Program.
If two trees score within five points of each other, they are designated co-champions.
In order to be considered a champion, co-champion, or to make the top five list for a tree species, the tree needs to rank highest with regards to tree height, crown spread, and trunk circumference.
The other champion trees at #ArlingtonNationalCemetery are the State Champion #RoyalPaulownia, State Champion #Yellowwood, State Co-Champion #SawtoothOak, and State Co-Champion #PinOak.
The #DwarfHackberry is in Section 8 and is estimated to be between 130-150 years old.
A #DwarfHackberry is multi-branched, with stiff twigs and smooth grey bark that becomes ridged and knotted as the plant ages. The Dwarf Hackberry fruit is orange-brown and globular with a sweet outer layer and contains a single seed.
A #DwarfHackberry, or Celtis tenuifolia, is a small tree or shrub in the elm family that typically grows three to 12 feet in height, but can reach up to 30 or more feet.
#ICYMI: The latest issue of @wdcgardener magazine featured our #DwarfHackberry being recently named a Virginia State Co-Champion tree! It joins four other champion and co-champion trees. Article available at https://t.co/ttTVwtZUXD. #HonorRememberExplore https://t.co/ttyFcGQI7b

Lt. Col. Juanita Redmond Hipps is laid to rest in Section 21, Grave 769-1. #HonorThem
Lt. Col. Juanita Redmond Hipps was one of the few nurses to escape and wrote "I Served on Bataan," a best seller in 1943, which later became the basis for the movie "So Proudly We Hail," a story about military nurses sent to the Philippines during WWII.
One of the Angels of Bataan, then Lt. Juanita Redmond served as a @USArmy nurse during #WorldWarII who served in Bataan and Corregidor. When Bataan and Corregidor fell, almost 80 nurses were captured and imprisoned.

Two years after the war ended, President Harry S. Truman signed an order to desegregate the U.S. Armed Forces, closing the book on the #BuffaloSoldiers.
As featured in the novel and film Miracle at St. Anna, the 92nd distinguished themselves on the battlefield, disproving skeptics and earning an honored chapter in the history of World War II.
The unit's distinctive insignia was a buffalo patch. The term "buffalo soldiers" dates to post-Civil War conflicts with Indians who granted the honorific to an all-black cavalry outfit. #BuffaloSoldiers served in the #SpanishAmericanWar, #WorldWarI, and #WorldWarII. https://t.co/d08jc9jKde
In Section 23 there is a Red Maple tree dedicated to the 92nd Infantry Division. Of the 3 African-American divisions activated during #WorldWarII, only the 92nd would serve as a full division in combat. Twenty-one is symbolic of the highest salute given to dignitaries in military and state ceremonies.
While on duty the sentinel crosses a 63-foot rubber surfaced walkway in exactly 21 steps. He then faces the Tomb for 21 seconds, turns again, and pauses an additional 21 seconds before retracing his steps.
Originally, a civilian watchman guarded the tomb and, then in 1926, a military guard from the Washington Provisional Brigade, guarded the tomb during daylight hours. In 1948, when the #OldGuard was reactivated, they assumed the responsibility of guarding the tomb.
Sentinels, all volunteers, are considered to be the best of the elite @USArmyOldGuard, the oldest active-duty infantry unit in the @USArmy serving our nation since 1784.

Stabilized the mast where rivets broke due to internal rusting of the steel.

Two children who died in their infancy are interred beside President Kennedy, as is his wife, Jacqueline Bouvier Kennedy Onassis.
President John Fitzgerald Kennedy was the 35th president of the United States for for two years, 10 months and two days, yet his grave remains among the most visited locations at Arlington. He is laid to rest in Section 45.
President William Howard Taft was interred in Arlington National Cemetery March 11, 1930. His widow, Helen Herron Taft, was buried beside him May 25, 1943.
President William Howard Taft was the first president to be buried in Arlington National Cemetery. He was also the only person to have served as president of the United States, and as chief justice of the United States on the U.S. Supreme Court.

Learn more about Tiffany Studios‚Äô mausoleums and mortuary monuments courtesy of the @MetMuseum‚Äôs Thomas J. Watson Library at https://t.co/QXZrNdCvzP.
In the late 1800s and early 1900s, Louis Comfort Tiffany‚Äôs studio also designed mausoleums, tombs, headstones and other mortuary monuments.
According to @TiffanyAndCo, Louis Comfort Tiffany was a world leader of the Art Nouveau movement. He is revered for his fanciful jewelry as well as his stained glass lamps and windows that brighten museums, banks, hospitals, hotel lobbies and more.
According to the @MorseMuseum, although his father, Charles Lewis Tiffany, had founded the most prestigious silver and jewelry company in America, @TiffanyAndCo, Louis Comfort Tiffany chose another professional direction.
Tiffany Studios was named for Louis Comfort Tiffany, who was born #onthisday in 1848. (Photo courtesy of @TiffanyAndCo) https://t.co/weY6CZMLMI

These men, known as the #TuskegeeAirmen, served with distinction in combat, and they contributed to the eventual integration of the U.S. armed services. (#USAirForce image) https://t.co/TFMxOAj5hB
An experiment in the @USAirForce, however, demonstrated conclusively that African-Americans -- if given equal opportunities and training -- could fly in, command and support combat units as well as anyone.
Reflecting American society and law at the time, the U.S. military remained racially segregated during #WorldWarII. Most African-American Soldiers and Sailors were restricted to labor battalions or other support positions. #BlackHistoryMonth

For more information on the destruction of the #USSMaine, visit @USNHistory's website at https://t.co/uQScFahiYJ.
The raising of the Maine took nearly two years, and from the wreck, 66 bodies were recovered. After refloating what remained of the Maine, the ship was towed out to sea and scuttled with full honors in water 600 fathoms deep March 16, 1912.
The Secretary of War also authorized and directed to remove the mast of the ship and place it upon a proper foundation in the cemetery.
All who perished in the disaster were buried in a cemetery in Havana. Congress approved a bill instructing that their remains be disinterred and transferred from Havana to #ArlingtonNationalCemetery March 30, 1898.
Nearly 3/4 of the ship's crew died as a result of the explosion. While the cause of the tragedy is still unsettled, American popular opinion blamed Spain, and war followed within a few months. #RememberTheMaine became the rallying cry. (Image courtesy of @NatlParkService) https://t.co/qhJq6JAhRO
Pictured is a @USNavy diving crew at work on the ship's wreck in 1898. (Photo courtesy of @USNHistory) https://t.co/XpXNCeYZAq
The Maine exploded on the night of Feb. 15, 1898. After all survivors were rescued, Capt. Charles Dwight Sigsbee reported the happenings by telegram to the Secretary of the @USNavy. (Scan of telegram courtesy of @USNatArchives) https://t.co/KMhrkgB981
American authorities believed that the presence of an American warship would be a stabilizing influence on the Spanish control of the colony and would assist in curtailing guerrilla activities.
Pictured is the #USSMaine passing Morro Castle as she enters Havana Harbor, Cuba, Jan. 25, 1898. (Original halftone photo courtesy of @USNHistory) https://t.co/2xZ1WxmKvx
#DidYouKnow in @NavalAcademy lore, the #USSMaine is claimed to be the longest ship in the @USNavy because the mainmast is here at #ArlingtonNationalCemetery (along with most of the crew) and the damaged foremast is at the #NavalAcademy?
The #USSMaineMemorial is bound on two sides by Sigsbee Drive, which is named after @USNavy Adm. Charles Dwight Sigsbee, who commanded the vessel as a captain at the time of its destruction. The foremast is at the U.S. @NavalAcademy in Annapolis, Maryland.  #HonorRememberExplore
#DidYouKnow that the main mast of the #USSMaine is here at #ArlingtonNationalCemetery? It is one of our many monuments and memorials, and is located in Section 24, across from the Memorial Amphitheater. #HonorRememberExplore (@USArmy file photo by Rachel Larue) https://t.co/WFUah4OGKO

Spottswood Poles is buried in Section 42, Grave 2324. #HonorThem
During #WorldWarI, Spottswood Poles was in the trenches for 191 days (the longest front-line service of any American regiment) with his unit, the @NationalGuardNY's 369th Infantry Regiment, also known as the #HarlemHellfighers, along with the @ArmeedeTerre's 4th Army.
Spottswood Poles was considered the finest player in the Negro Baseball League in the early 1900s, and was often referred to as the black Ty Cobb. In 1914, Poles' recorded batting average was .487.
It's #SpringTraining time for the @MLB! Have you ever heard of Spottswood Poles? Although a combat veteran with five battle stars and the #PurpleHeart, he is more well-known for his baseball career. (Photo courtesy of the @LibraryCongress) #BlackHistoryMonth #HonorThem https://t.co/xd5Jt9Z15v

.@USNavy Vice Adm. Alexander G. Lyle  retired Aug. 1, 1948, and passed away July 15, 1955. He is buried at #ArlingtonNationalCemetery in Section 2, Grave 11141-1-2. #HonorThem
Under heavy shellfire on the French front, April 23, 1918, @USNavy Vice Adm. Alexander G. Lyle rushed to the assistance of Cpl. Thomas Regan, who was seriously wounded, and administered such effective surgical aid while bombardment was still continuing, as to save Regan's life.
.@USNavy Vice Adm. Alexander G. Lyle earned the Medal of Honor for extraordinary heroism and devotion to duty while serving with the @USMC @Fighting_Fifth Regiment.

Gen. Omar Bradley is buried in Section 30, Grave 428-1-2. #HonorThem
Gen. Omar Bradley passed away April 8, 1981, just a few minutes after receiving an award from the @SocSciInstitute. He was buried in #ArlingtonNationalCemetery April 14, 1981, with full military honors, as the nation mourned the passing of this great and noble warrior. #HonorThem
Gen. Omar Bradley was the only Chairman to attain the five-star rank. He served as the Chairman until his retirement from military service in 1953.
Gen. Omar Bradley later became the first Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff in August 1949. He was promoted to the rank of General of the @USArmy Sept. 22, 1950.
At the time of the photo, Gen. George C. Marshall was the @USArmy's Chief of Staff. Gen. Dwight D. Eisenhower was Supreme Commander, Allied Expeditionary Forces, and Gen. Omar Bradley was commanding the 12th Army Group. Eisenhower became Chief of Staff in 1945, Bradley in 1948.
#OnThisDay in 1948, Gen. Omar Bradley succeeded Gen. Dwight Eisenhower as the @USArmy's Chief of Staff. Pictured are Gen. George C. Marshall, Gen. Dwight D. Eisenhower and Bradley in Verdun, France, 1944, during #WorldWarII. (File photo courtesy of @USArmyCMH) https://t.co/WTVtHyGtoH

#DidYouKnow that today is #FourChaplainsDay? Shown is #ChaplainsHill, where monuments are dedicated to the memory of chaplains who have served in the U.S. Armed Forces. (#USArmy photo by Elizabeth Fraser, Arlington National Cemetery) #HonorRememberExplore https://t.co/xHQ79L4T0H
#HonorThem https://t.co/uuSiFIdPqy
The inscription at that time simply read "Army and Navy Nurses." Authorization was granted in 1970, and then in 1971, the monument was rededicated to the service of @USAirForce, @USArmy and @USNavy nurses.
On July 13, 1970, @USNavy Capt. Delores Cornelius, deputy director of the #NavyNurseCorps, requested authority to install a bronze plaque over the existing inscription on the Nurses Memorial.
The granite statue of a nurse in uniform, sculptured by Frances Rich, honors the nurses who served in the U.S. armed forces in #WorldWarI, many of whom rest among the  hundreds of nurses buried in Section 21, commonly known as the "Nurses Section."
Against a background of evergreens, the heroic size white marble figure looks out upon the @USAirForce, @USArmy and @USNavy nurses who so valiantly earned their right to lie at Arlington.
#HappyBirthday to the @ArmyNurseCorps! #OnThisDay 117 years ago, the #ArmyNurseCorps became a permanent corps of the Medical Department. Pictured is the #NursesMemorial in Section 21. RT @ArmyChiefStaff: From the Boston Massacre to present-day operations, African-Americans have served to defend our nation #InTimesOfWar. D‚Ä¶
Learn more about James Parks at https://t.co/3ZLe9C0kdc. #HonorThem
When Parks died on Aug. 21, 1929, he was granted special permission to be buried at #ArlingtonNationalCemetery. He is buried in Section 15, Grave 2.
Parks served in the @USArmy from 1861 to 1929 by working as a grave digger and maintenance man for the cemetery.
Parks was freed in 1862 under the terms of the will of his former owner, George Washington Parke Custis. He still lived on Arlington Estate when Secretary of War Edwin M. Stanton signed the orders designating Arlington as a military burial ground.
The first graves in Arlington National Cemetery were dug by James Parks and without him, the story of Arlington would be incomplete.
#OnThisDay in 1865, Congress passed the 13th Amendment to the U.S. Constitution, which abolished slavery. Pictured is James Parks, a former slave who is the only person buried in #ArlingtonNationalCemetery who was born on the property. Photo courtesy of @NatlParkService. https://t.co/jSeWInR4Ms

The @ArlingtonNPS, or the Robert E. Lee Memorial, was built by George Washington Parke Custis in 1802-1818, and was the home of Robert E. Lee from 1831-1861.
It is named for Gen. George Brinton McClellan, first commander of the Army of the Potomac during the American #CivilWar. While the Army of the Potomac was being organized, its headquarters was located in the #ArlingtonHouse.
Construction on the gate, made from Red Seneca Sandstone, was completed in 1879.

The battle that ensued is known historically as the #BattleOfTheBulge. The courage and fortitude of the American Soldier was tested against great adversity. Nevertheless, the quality of his response ultimately meant the victory of freedom over tyranny.
On Dec. 16, 1944, with the onset of winter, the German army launched a counteroffensive that was intended to cut through the Allied forces in a manner that would turn the tide of the war in Hitler's favor.
In late 1944, during the wake of the Allied forces' successful D-Day invasion of Normandy, France, it seemed as if the #WorldWarII was all but over.

Lt. Col. Juanita Hipps is buried in Section 21. #NationalReadingDay
Lt. Col. Juanita Hipps was a @USArmy nurse in #WorldWarII, serving in hospitals in Bataan and Corregidor. She was one of eight nurses who escaped just before the surrender of the Philippine Islands. #NationalReadingDay
Lt. Col. Juanita Hipps, a native of Swansea, South Carolina, wrote "I Serve on Bataan," which was a best seller in 1943. The book became the basis for the movie "So Proudly We Hail." #NationalReadingDay https://t.co/sH8kNKZ4An
Samuel Dashiell Hammett is buried in Section 12. #NationalReadingDay
Samuel Dashiell Hammett also wrote numerous detective novels, including The Maltese Falcon and The Thin Man in 1930s. He was jailed during McCarthy era as communist threat. #NationalReadingDay
Samuel Dashiell Hammett, who is originally from Maryland, served in the @USArmy during #WorldWarI and #WorldWarII and reached the rank of Technician, Third Grade. #NationalReadingDay https://t.co/sKJfeu3pa8
Mary Roberts Rinehart is buried in Section 3. #NationalReadingDay
Mary Roberts Rinehart wrote short stories, mysteries, romances and was a correspondent from the front during #WorldWarI for the @satevepost. #NationalReadingDay
Mary Roberts Rinehart is also credited with creating the "had I only known" form of mysteries and with the phrase "the butler did it" even though she never really used it in any of her books. #NationalReadingDay
Mary Roberts Rinehart is credited as being the American Agatha Christie, though she began writing mysteries 14 years prior. #NationalReadingDay https://t.co/N8SIbUbWhx

@USArmy .@virginia_tech manages the Virginia Big Tree Program and @ArlingtonVA manages a more local Arlington County Champion Tree Program.
@USArmy If two trees score within five points of each other, they are designated co-champions.
@USArmy In order to be considered a champion, co-champion, or to make the top five list for a tree species, the tree needs to rank highest with regards to tree height, crown spread, and trunk circumference.
@USArmy The other champion trees at ANC are the State Champion #RoyalPaulownia, State Champion #Yellowwood, State Co-Champion #SawtoothOak, and State Co-Champion #PinOak.
@USArmy #DidYouKnow that Arlington is home to five Virginia State Champion and Co-Champion trees?
@USArmy A Dwarf Hackberry is multi-branched, with stiff twigs and smooth grey bark that becomes ridged and knotted as the plant ages. The Dwarf Hackberry fruit is orange-brown and globular with a sweet outer layer and contains a single seed.
@USArmy A Dwarf Hackberry, or Celtis tenuifolia, is a small tree or shrub in the elm family that typically grows three to 12 feet in height, but can reach up to 30 or more feet.
@USArmy The #DwarfHackberry is in Section 8 and is estimated to be between 130-150 years old.

Medgar Evers is buried in Section 36, Grave 1431. 		#HonorThem https://t.co/0K0Yq8fSlU
Information for this thread courtesy of Minot Air Force Base - see the original at https://t.co/rZuK0zNi76.
In 1970, Medgar Evers College (@NewsAtMedgar) was created in Brooklyn, New York, as part of the City University of New York, or @CUNY.
Mickey Levine, former chairman of the American Veterans Committee said at the services, "no soldier in this field has fought more courageously, more heroically than Medgar Evers."
Evers was buried on June 19, 1963, in #ArlingtonNationalCemetery and received full military honors in front of a crowd of more than 2,000 people.
On June 12, 1963, Evers shot outside of his home after returning from an integration meeting.
In December 1954, Evers became the @NAACP's first field officer in Mississippi.
When his application was rejected on grounds of race, Evers became the focus of an @NAACP campaign to desegregate the school, a case aided by the U.S. Supreme Court ruling in the case of Brown v. Board of Education 347 US 483 that segregation was unconstitutional.
Evers applied to the then-segregated @olemisslaw in February 1954.
Evers married classmate Myrlie Beasley Dec. 24, 1951, and completed work on his degree the following year.
Despite his resentment over such treatment, Evers enrolled at @AlcornStateU, majoring in business administration.
Despite fighting for his country as part of the Battle of Normandy, Evers soon found that his skin color gave him no freedom when he and five friends were forced away at gunpoint from voting in a local election.
A native of Decatur, Mississippi, Evers attended school there until being inducted into the @USArmy in 1943.
While he is sometimes overshadowed by other prominent civil rights leaders, his legacy and achievements continue to this day.
Medgar Evers was a #WorldWarII veteran, a student athlete, a distinguished Mississippi businessman and a pioneer of the civil rights movement.
#DidYouKnow some states across the country celebrate #CivilRightsDay each year on the third Monday in January to honor civil rights activists? 		Today, we are honoring Medgar Evers. https://t.co/ajTmluqoLu

To learn more about Dr. King and his legacy, follow @TheKingCenter or visit https://t.co/X0YLkelZss.
If you're looking for ways for you and your family to participate in the this national day of service, visit https://t.co/O54CY99E4h.
The #MLKDay of Service is the only federal holiday observed as a national day of service:  a "day on, not a day off."
In 1994, #Congress designated the Martin Luther King Jr. federal holiday as a national day of service and charged the Corporation for National and Community Service with leading the effort.
#MLKDay is observed on the third Monday of January each year.
In 1983, Congress passed legislation that established a federal holiday to commemorate the birthday of Rev. Dr. Martin Luther King, Jr.  He was assassinated in 1968. #MLK50Forward https://t.co/d8GDAH89oH
